\hypertarget{preface}{%
\section*{Preface}\label{preface}}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Preface}

This document is a set of notes (and perhaps later a textbook) for a course on data science methods for graduate students in social and behavioral sciences, taught by Professor Dan Hicks (they/them), UC Merced.

The course overview (including reading and lab assignments) is available here: \url{https://docs.google.com/spreadsheets/d/1iS_I-LgT7bBRBUOYEOyJNHBe7Q8X_c3B1E9jap4b6jw/edit?usp=sharing}

PDF versions of each chapter can be found here: \href{handouts/index.html}{Handouts}

(The URLs to the handouts are buggy. To get to them, look for \texttt{handout/handout/} in the URL; delete the second \texttt{handout/}.)

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

These notes were written in \href{http://www.rstudio.com/ide/}{RStudio} using \href{http://bookdown.org/}{bookdown}. The complete source is available on \href{https://github.com/data-science-methods/book/}{GitHub} and is automatically rebuilt using \href{https://github.com/data-science-methods/book/}{Travis CI}.

This version of the book was built with R version 4.0.2 (2020-06-22), \href{https://pandoc.org/}{pandoc} version 2.10, and the following packages:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}

\hypertarget{setting-up}{%
\section*{Setting up}\label{setting-up}}
\addcontentsline{toc}{section}{Setting up}

Before the first day of class, you'll need to set up a few things: \textbf{R, RStudio, Slack, Zoom, and a GitHub account}. I'd also like you to fill out a \textbf{pre-class survey}. R is the primary piece of software we'll be learning to use. RStudio is an IDE (integrated development environment) for using R. (Think of R as the engine of a car and RStudio as the dashboard, steering wheel, and pedals.) Slack will be the main way we communicate; we'll use Zoom for class time. GitHub is a service for sharing and collaborating on software engineering projects.

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\caption{Key links for setup}\tabularnewline
\toprule
\endhead
R & \url{https://cran.r-project.org/}\tabularnewline
RStudio & \url{https://rstudio.com/products/rstudio/download/}\tabularnewline
Slack & \url{http://datascienceme-ke13588.slack.com/}\tabularnewline
GitHub & \url{https://github.com/}; \url{https://github.com/data-science-methods}\tabularnewline
Survey & \url{https://ucmerced.az1.qualtrics.com/jfe/form/SV_eVU8JWREUu4UNH7}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

I'll assume you have R and RStudio installed using either \emph{the cloud way} or \emph{the Mac way}. If your computer is running Windows, Linux, or some other operating system that isn't Mac OS, I'm going to assume you have things set up the cloud way. These other operating systems are perfectly fine. But I don't use them, which makes it much harder for me to help you if things aren't working. And \href{https://www.jimhester.com/post/2020-08-20-best-os-for-r/}{Windows handles things like dependencies, file paths, text file formats in idiosyncratic ways that can cause weird errors}.

In either case, all of the software and services we'll be using are either free or freemium. You won't need any of the paid extras for this course.

\hypertarget{a-note-on-accessibility}{%
\subsection{A Note on Accessibility}\label{a-note-on-accessibility}}

I chose the tools and platforms for this course in part because they're industry-standard. If you pursue a career as a data scientist in industry, you'll be expected to use Slack and Github on a daily basis. And RStudio is by far the most commonly used IDE (``integrated development environment'') for R.

However, like many other technologies, they were originally developed using ableist assumptions about ``normal'' computer users. In response to criticism, the developers of these systems and tools have gone back and made their technologies more accessible. But there may still be barriers to accessibility that I have not anticipated. And of course the pandemic has created additional barriers, such as dependency on screensharing.

If you encounter a barrier to participating in this course --- even a small inconvenience --- please let me know. Similarly, if you have ideas for making the course more accessible, please share them with me.

\hypertarget{hardware-recommendations}{%
\subsection{Hardware Recommendations}\label{hardware-recommendations}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  I strongly recommend \emph{against} tablets (iPad, Surface) for this course
\item
  If you use RStudio Cloud, you have more flexibility for hardware. You can probably get away with using a netbook (Chromebook) if you take this approach.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Though see the note below about usage limits for RStudio Cloud.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Otherwise, at the start of the semester, I recommend that your machine have at least

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    200 GB free hard drive space
  \item
    8 GB RAM
  \item
    Intel i5 processor
  \item
    Operating system capable of running R 4.0.0

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Mac: 10.13 (High Sierra) or more recent
    \item
      Windows: See \href{https://cran.r-project.org/bin/windows/base/rw-FAQ.html\#Does-R-run-under-Windows-Vista_003f}{here} or consider just using RStudio Cloud
    \item
      If you're running Linux I'm going to assume you can figure this out yourself; or consider just using RStudio Cloud
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{r-and-rstudio}{%
\subsection{R and RStudio}\label{r-and-rstudio}}

\hypertarget{the-mac-way}{%
\subsubsection{The Mac Way}\label{the-mac-way}}

You can find some notes on accessibility and RStudio here: \url{https://support.rstudio.com/hc/en-us/articles/360044226673-RStudio-Accessibility-Features}.

With this way, R and RStudio are installed and run on your computer, and all of the data files that you work with will also be on your computer. Unless you're running a Chromebook or similar, your computer is almost certainly more powerful than the default instance that RStudio Cloud gives you (see below), and you can use familiar tools to do things like organize folders and preview files. But sometimes particular details about the way your computer is set up can cause R and RStudio to do weird things, and these problems can be very difficult to diagnose and solve.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Go to \url{https://cran.r-project.org/}. Download and install the latest release of R for your operating system (which should be Mac OS if you're following this way). As of 2020-06-02, the latest release is 4.0.0.
\item
  Go to \href{https://rstudio.com/products/rstudio/download/\#download}{\texttt{rstudio.com}}. Download and install the latest release of RStudio Desktop for your operating system (which should be Mac OS if you're following this way). As of 2020-06-02, the latest release is 1.3.959.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{the-cloud-way}{%
\subsubsection{The Cloud Way}\label{the-cloud-way}}

(\textbf{Note 2017-07-07} Starting in August, RStudio Cloud is moving out of beta and becoming a regular subscription service. Information on pricing is at \url{https://rstudio.cloud/plans/free}. We'll have to try it out, but it seems likely that the Free tier won't give you enough compute time (``project hours'') for this course. I can subscribe to the Instructor tier, but that has its own limitations. We'll have to see how things go.)

With this way, R and RStudio are installed and run on virtual computers (called \emph{instances}) on a big server somewhere. Each project can have its own instance, meaning that it runs on a nice clean computer and shouldn't have any weirdness. The RStudio Cloud service is currently in beta; it's free to use, but not completely guaranteed to be free of bugs. In order to use the service, you have to be connected to the internet, and you'll have to use the RStudio interface or a terminal window to do things like move files around. Also, the instances aren't very powerful, and so they'll be slow if you try to work with large data sets or do a complex analysis.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Go to \href{https://rstudio.cloud/}{\texttt{rstudio.cloud}}. Look for ``Sign Up'' in the upper right.\footnote{RStudio gives you the option to log in with Google or GitHub. As a general practice, I recommend always keeping your accounts separate. Use a password manager to generate separate, strong passwords for each site. I use \href{https://www.lastpass.com/}{LastPass}.}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{slack}{%
\subsection{Slack}\label{slack}}

For Fall 2020, the class Slack channel is at \url{https://datascienceme-ke13588.slack.com}. Please use your UC Merced email to sign up.

If you use a screenreader, here are some notes on accessibility and Slack: \url{https://www.marcozehe.de/status-of-the-accessibility-of-slack/}.

\hypertarget{github}{%
\subsection{GitHub}\label{github}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Go to \href{https://github.com/}{\texttt{github.com}}. You can create a new account right on the front page. I don't care what email address you use here.
\item
  You can fill in your profile if you want.
\item
  You'll send me the email address associated with your Github account in the pre-class survey.
\end{enumerate}

NB If you're using Windows, you'll need to \href{https://gitforwindows.org/}{download and install Git}.

\hypertarget{screen-layouts}{%
\subsection{Screen Layouts}\label{screen-layouts}}

In this course, a significant amount of time will be spent on \emph{live coding}. This means that I (or whoever's doing the lecturing) will be writing code in real time, and you'll follow along, transcribing the code into RStudio (or whatever) on your local machine and running it at the same time as me.

Live coding is a great way to learn coding practices. But it requires you to have quick access to 3-5 different things:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  My screenshare (so you can see what I'm typing)
\item
  The course Slack (so you can ask for help or other questions, or make comments)
\item
  Possibly a separate way to put up a flag so that I can see immediately that you have a question
\item
  Your own workspace (RStudio or whatever)
\item
  Possibly a separate app for taking notes
\end{itemize}

In an ordinary classroom, the first three things would all be handled by the classroom environment. In a fully online setting, everything needs to be handled on your computer.

The most effective way to do this is to have multiple displays. This is also the most expensive.

\href{https://carpentries.org/blog/2020/06/online-workshop-logistics-and_screen-layouts/}{This blog post} discusses alternatives to multiple displays. In particular, the window layout below fits the screenshare, chat, and your workspace on to a single laptop display. You can also have a separate text file open in RStudio for notes. You might consider having Slack open on your phone --- so that you can keep an eye on the chat --- as well in the background on your computer --- so that you can alt-tab to it to actually type things.

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/layout} 

}

\caption{Example of a multiwindow layout for following live coding via Zoom. Source: `carpentries.org`, <https://bit.ly/2YFh9Ea>}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-2}
\end{figure}

\hypertarget{introduction}{%
\section{Introduction}\label{introduction}}

\hypertarget{land-acknowledgement}{%
\subsection{Land Acknowledgement}\label{land-acknowledgement}}

UC Merced and the City of Merced are on the traditional territory of the Yokut people. This land was stolen by Spanish, Mexican, and American settlers through acts of slavery and genocide. In addition, UC Merced is strongly associated with Ahwahne, known as Yosemite Valley. This valley was the traditional home of the Ahwahnechee people, who were the victims of some especially horrific, state-sponsored genocidal acts. For more on the history of Ahwahne, see \url{https://tinyurl.com/y879jw8s}. For more information on land acknowledgments, see \url{https://native-land.ca}.

\hypertarget{about-me}{%
\subsection{About me}\label{about-me}}

Dan Hicks is a philosopher turned data scientist turned philosopher.

I use they/them pronouns and identify as nonbinary. I grew up in Placerville, about two hours north of Merced in the Sierra Foothills. One branch of my family came to California during the Gold Rush, so I identify heavily as a Californian and have some complicated feelings about the genocide. I finished my PhD in philosophy of science at Notre Dame in 2012. After that I worked in a series of research positions in academia and the federal government. During 2015-2019 I was using data science methods at least half-time. I joined the faculty at UC Merced in Fall 2019.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Email: \href{mailto:dhicks4@ucmerced.edu}{\nolinkurl{dhicks4@ucmerced.edu}}
\item
  Student hours: By appointment: \url{https://doodle.com/mm/danhicks/office-hours}
\item
  Website: \url{https://dhicks.github.io/}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-this-course-isnt-and-is}{%
\subsection{What this course isn't, and is}\label{what-this-course-isnt-and-is}}

Is Not:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  a statistics course (in the way you think)
\item
  a general introduction to software engineering
\item
  a basic introduction to R? (Unless that's what you all need?)
\item
  carefully planned out from beginning to end
\end{itemize}

Is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  an introduction to data science
\item
  about data cleaning, data management, and exploratory data analysis
\item
  habituation to some good software engineering practices that are especially valuable for data science work
\item
  in the \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_release_life_cycle\#Pre-alpha}{pre-alpha stage of development}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{learning-outcomes}{%
\subsection{Learning outcomes}\label{learning-outcomes}}

By the end of the course, students will be able to

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  apply concepts from software engineering and philosophy of science to methodological decisions in data science, including

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    reproducibility vs.~robustness vs.~replicability
  \item
    value and hazards of open data
  \item
    thick data (Wang \protect\hyperlink{ref-WangWhyBigData2016}{2016})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  use exploratory data analysis techniques and tools to identify potential data errors and potential phenomena for further analysis
\item
  clean raw data and produce a reproducible codebook for both downstream analysis and public release of data according to FAIR standards (Wilkinson et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-WilkinsonFAIRGuidingPrinciples2016}{2016})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    {[}create and use a cloud database to work with big data{]}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  manage data, analysis, and outputs for reproducibility (assembling a project compendium; Gentleman and Lang \protect\hyperlink{ref-GentlemanStatisticalAnalysesReproducible2004}{2004}) using best practices of data handling, a clear directory structure (Wilson et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-WilsonGoodEnoughPractices2017}{2017}), self-documenting code, version control, build automation, {[}and continuous integration{]}
\item
  apply philosophical and data science concepts to integrated ethical-technical analysis to case studies in algorithmic injustice

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    understand the background on the COMPAS case: Angwin et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-AngwinMachineBiasThere2016}{2016}); Corbett-Davies et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-Corbett-DaviesComputerProgramUsed2016}{2016})
  \item
    reproduce several fairness statistics on COMPAS data
  \item
    explain tradeoffs between false negatives and false positives in terms of inductive risk
  \item
    explain Kleinberg, Mullainathan, and Raghavan (\protect\hyperlink{ref-KleinbergInherentTradeOffsFair2016}{2016}) impossibility result (not necessarily the proof) and show that it applies to the COMPAS datas
  \item
    critically discuss profiling and thresholds (Pierson et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-PiersonLargescaleAnalysisRacial2020}{2020}) as potential explanation for differences in ``base rates'' of recidivism
  \item
    critically locate discussions of fairness and recidivism in a broader social context, and identify implications for working data scientists (Selbst et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-SelbstFairnessAbstractionSociotechnical2018}{2018}; Hoffmann \protect\hyperlink{ref-HoffmannWhereFairnessFails2019}{2019}; Hanna et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-HannaCriticalRaceMethodology2019}{2019})
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{workflow}{%
\subsection{Workflow}\label{workflow}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The first \textasciitilde10 weeks of the course will focus on skill development, with a mix of livecoding lectures and labs.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Labs will be set up to give you automated feedback.\\
  \item
    There will probably be asynchronous discussion using Slack for some of the labs.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  On November 10 we'll be working on project with Hanna Gunn's Data Ethics course.\\
\item
  The last few weeks of the course will be devoted to term projects.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Tentative idea for this: go find some data; clean it, explore it; produce a codebook and reproducible exploratory data analysis
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{notes-on-some-course-design-decisions}{%
\subsection{Notes on some course design decisions}\label{notes-on-some-course-design-decisions}}

\hypertarget{r-not-python}{%
\subsubsection{R, not Python}\label{r-not-python}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Python is a perfectly good language for general programming

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    And has some advantages over R in certain data science aspects, including working with strings and developing custom data structures
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

However,

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  R is a better language for doing data science (Shotwell \protect\hyperlink{ref-ShotwellWhyUse2019}{2019})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Vectors, data frames, and common statistical models are installed and loaded by default
  \item
    R is more functional (Wu \protect\hyperlink{ref-WuPythonVsChoosing2019}{2019}; Paleologo \protect\hyperlink{ref-PaleologoWhatDifferenceMachine2018}{2018})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  R is also better for beginners

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://twitter.com/kccarrell/status/1030482372775620613}{CRAN}
  \item
    RStudio
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{tidyverse-r-not-base-r}{%
\subsubsection{Tidyverse R, not base R}\label{tidyverse-r-not-base-r}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  ``Base R'' refers to the central code for R and the packages included when you install it

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Base R is universal and free of additional dependencies. Code written entirely in base R will run on any other R installation (with the same version).
  \item
    However, because the R Core Development Team has always thought backwards-compatibility is important, base R has some idiosyncracies (Keyes \protect\hyperlink{ref-KeyesRbitraryStandards2016}{2016})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{tidyverse} is a suite of packages developed by the manufacturers of RStudio

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A very useful set of general tools can be loaded simply by calling \texttt{library(tidyverse)}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      And some more specialized tools are also installed as part of the suite
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Tidyverse is designed to mitigate a lot of the base R idiosyncracies
  \item
    While making code more human-readable and easier to reason about
  \item
    However, several of its tools use something called ``non-standard evaluation'' (NSE).

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      NSE makes tidyverse code easier to write for beginners, because you don't have to think as much about where exactly your variables are located
    \item
      At the same time, NSE creates problems for intermediate coders, because passing tidyverse-style ``bare names'' through function arguments causes cryptic errors
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{scripts-not-notebooks}{%
\subsubsection{Scripts, not notebooks}\label{scripts-not-notebooks}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Quite a few scientists like the way notebooks mix together code, visualizations, tables, and text (Perkel \protect\hyperlink{ref-PerkelWhyJupyterData2018}{2018})
\end{itemize}

However,

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Notebooks do not play nicely with version control (Ahemad \protect\hyperlink{ref-AhemadVersionControlJupyter2018}{2018}; Whitmore \protect\hyperlink{ref-WhitmoreJupyterNotebookBest2015}{2015})
\item
  Notebooks encourage bad software engineering habits and discourage good ones (Grus \protect\hyperlink{ref-GrusDonNotebooksJoel}{n.d.}; Hiltch \protect\hyperlink{ref-HiltchJupyterNotebookCancer2019}{2019})
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-is-this-thing-called-data-science}{%
\section{What is this thing called data science?}\label{what-is-this-thing-called-data-science}}

\hypertarget{standard-definition}{%
\subsection{Standard definition}\label{standard-definition}}



\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/Abisiga} 

}

\caption{Data science, defined as the intersection of CS, stats, and ``business knowledge.'' Source: \url{https://www.kdnuggets.com/2020/08/top-10-lists-data-science.html}}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-4}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The intersection of computer science/software engineering, statistics, and ``business knowledge''
\end{itemize}

Or, more concretely,



\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/Boykis} 

}

\caption{Boykis Twitter poll of ``data scientists''; 67\% said they spend most of their time cleaning data/moving data. Source: \url{https://twitter.com/vboykis/status/1089920316644704256}}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-5}
\end{figure}



\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/Scott} 

}

\caption{Scott tweet: ``One of the biggest failures I see in junior ML/CV engineers is a complete lack of interest in building data sets. While it is boring grunt work I think there is so much to be learned in putting together a dataset. It is like half the problem.'' Source: \url{https://twitter.com/kscottz/status/1091423467772162049}}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-6}
\end{figure}



\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/Hudon} 

}

\caption{Hudon tweet containing a graphic labelled ``Full Stack Data Science,'' with categories ``data engineer'' (entries collect, build data pipeline), ``data analyst'' (entries reporting, data viz), ``data scientist'' (entries build models, run statistical tests), and ``machine learning engineer'' (entries deploy model, build model). Source: \url{https://twitter.com/beeonaposy/status/1298070348844457987}}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-7}
\end{figure}

However,

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  This focuses on \emph{tools and techniques}, not the epistemic and practical \emph{goals} of data science

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    An ecologist spends most of their time collecting specimens in the field and processing them in a lab, vs.~
  \item
    An ecologist studies interactions among organisms and their environment
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{a-prior-question-what-is-this-thing-called-data}{%
\subsection{A prior question: What is this thing called ``data''?}\label{a-prior-question-what-is-this-thing-called-data}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Representational view}: Data are ``reliable representations of reality which are produced via the interaction between humans and the world''

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    ``The production of data is equivalent to `capturing' features of the world that can be used for systematic study''
  \item
    ``Data {[}are seen as{]} as `raw' products of research, which are as close as it gets to unmediated knowledge of reality''
  \item
    Data provide ``an objective foundation for the acquisition of knowledge'' (Leonelli \protect\hyperlink{ref-LeonelliScientificResearchBig2020}{2020})
  \item
    \textbf{Data become data when they're produced according to a reliable, standardized measurement process}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Relational view}: ``Data are objects that are treated as potential or actual evidence for scientific claims in ways that can, at least in principle, be scrutinised and accounted for''

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    ``Any object can be used as a datum, or stop being used as such, depending on the circumstances''
  \item
    \textbf{Data become data when they're used as evidence} (Leonelli \protect\hyperlink{ref-LeonelliScientificResearchBig2020}{2020})
  \item
    Note implications for ideas of ``unmediated knowledge'' and ``objective foundation''
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{back-to-data-science}{%
\subsection{Back to ``data science''}\label{back-to-data-science}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Data science ≠ lab science (often)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Data collection isn't standardized

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Found (or bought), not made
    \item
      Data as byproduct
    \item
      This is the point of the tweets above
    \item
      ex: scraping from Twitter, Wikipedia, administrative records
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Remixing and recombining data

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      ex: pesticide use + Census data in Hicks (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCensusDemographicsChlorpyrifos2020}{2020}\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCensusDemographicsChlorpyrifos2020}{a})
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Data first, questions later
  \item
    \textbf{Relational view is often a better fit}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The goal of data science is to \textbf{produce data}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    To create, transform, and juxtapose objects such that they can be used as evidence
  \item
    Can be done experimentally in a lab, but in other ways too
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{part-some-fundamental-tools}{%
\part{Some fundamental tools}\label{part-some-fundamental-tools}}

\hypertarget{git-and-version-control}{%
\section{Git and Version Control}\label{git-and-version-control}}

\hypertarget{some-motivating-examples}{%
\subsection{Some motivating examples}\label{some-motivating-examples}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  While working on your analysis code, you accidentally delete the first 35 lines of the script. You only discover this three days later, when you restart R and try to run the script from the top. Because of the time you last half of your senior thesis, you've been saving every couple of minutes.\\
\item
  You're working on a paper with two coauthors. You prepare the final draft to send for submission: \texttt{paper\ final.docx}. But one of your coauthors discovers a typo. Now it's \texttt{paper\ final\ fixed\ typo.docx}. Another realizes six references are missing. \texttt{paper\ final\ fixed\ typo\ refs.docx}. That's getting confusing so you change it to \texttt{paper\ 2\ Aug\ 2021.docx}. Once it comes back from review you need to make revisions. Now you have \texttt{paper\ 30\ Jan\ 2022.docx} and, after your collaborators make their changes, \texttt{paper\ 12\ February\ 2022\ DJH.docx} and \texttt{paper\ 12\ February\ 20222\ final.docx}.
\item
  You have a complicated analysis spread over several scripts. You want to explore a variant analysis, but doing so will involve changes in 15 different places across 3 different files. You're not sure if this variant analysis will work; you may or may not want to keep it.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{version-control}{%
\subsection{Version control}\label{version-control}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Basic idea: Tools for tracking and reversing changes to code over time
\item
  Useful for identifying and reversing breaking changes
\item
  Implementations upload to cloud, track who contributes code, control who can suggest vs.~actually change code
\item
  Good for collaboration, publishing code
\item
  git

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    One of many version control systems
  \item
    Very popular in part thanks to GitHub, which provided free hosting for open-source projects

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      In April 2020, GitHub also made private/closed-source repositories free
    \item
      Resources for students (and teachers): \url{https://education.github.com/}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{how-to-do-things-with-git}{%
\subsection{How to do things with git}\label{how-to-do-things-with-git}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Getting help

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{git\ fetch\ -h}
  \item
    On Mac/Unix: \texttt{man\ git-fetch} (hit \texttt{q} to exit)
  \item
    On Windows: \texttt{git\ help\ fetch}
  \item
    A good cheat sheet: \url{https://www.atlassian.com/git/tutorials/atlassian-git-cheatsheet}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Interfaces

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Command line
  \item
    \href{https://support.rstudio.com/hc/en-us/articles/200526207-Using-Projects}{RStudio projects}
  \item
    GUIs: \href{https://desktop.github.com/}{GitHub}, \href{https://www.sourcetreeapp.com/}{Sourcetree}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Setup

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://git-scm.com/book/en/v2/Getting-Started-Installing-Git}{Installation}
  \item
    User config
  \end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git config --global user.name "Maria Hernandez"
$ git config --global user.email mhernandez123@ucmerced.edu
$ git config --global core.editor "nano"
\end{verbatim}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    You don't have to use \texttt{nano}, but it's the best option for beginners. For more on text editor options, see here \url{https://git-scm.com/book/en/v2/Appendix-C\%3A-Git-Commands-Setup-and-Config\#_core_editor}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{git\ init}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Create a folder called \texttt{test} and open a terminal window there.\\
  \end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git status
$ git init
$ git status
\end{verbatim}
\item
  \texttt{add} and \texttt{commit} a file
  Create a text file and save it as \texttt{readme.txt} in \texttt{test}.\\
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git status
$ git add test.txt
$ git status
$ git commit -m "my first commit"
$ git status
\end{verbatim}

After adding a file, you can add all changes to tracked files using \texttt{-u}:

\begin{verbatim}
$ git status
$ git add -u
$ git status
$ git commit -m "-u is a shortcut"
\end{verbatim}

NB You always need to \texttt{add} and then \texttt{commit}. Doing just one by itself doesn't do much.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{.gitignore}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Create a subfolder \texttt{images} and put an image file of some kind in it.
  \item
    Create a text file \texttt{.gitignore} in \texttt{test}. Put the following line in:
  \end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
images
\end{verbatim}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Add another image file. Edit \texttt{.gitignore} so that it ignores the first image file but not the second.\\
  \item
    Track \texttt{.gitignore}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{working-with-github-remotes}{%
\subsection{Working with GitHub remotes}\label{working-with-github-remotes}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{remote}: A copy of a repository that lives on a server somewhere else
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{working-with-your-own-repos}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Working with \emph{your own} repos}{Working with your own repos}}\label{working-with-your-own-repos}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  On GitHub, click ``New'' and walk through the steps to create a new repository. Let's call it \texttt{mytest}.
\item
  Copy the URL: \texttt{https://github.com/username/mytest}
\item
  Back in the Terminal window:
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git remote add origin https://github.com/username/mytest
\end{verbatim}

You'll need to authenticate with your GitHub password. If you forget the exact incantation, try \texttt{git\ push}; it will give you a helpful example.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{push} and then refresh GitHub in your browser
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git push
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Edit the repository (eg, use the prompt near the bottom to create a readme). Then:
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git fetch  ## Downloads a comparison, but doesn't change your local files yet
$ git status
$ git pull   ## Changes your local files
$ git status
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{working-with-someone-elses-repos}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Working with \emph{someone else's} repos}{Working with someone else's repos}}\label{working-with-someone-elses-repos}}

GitHub lets you download someone else's repo (``clone''), and modify it locally, but \emph{not} upload directly. You can suggest a change to someone else's code by submitting a \emph{pull request}, which requires \emph{forking} the repository.

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/fork-and-clone} 

}

\caption{*Forking* copies a repository to your GitHub account.  Then you *clone* the copy to your local machine.  You can *push* to your remote copy as usual.  You can suggest changes to the original using a *pull request*.  Source: <https://happygitwithr.com/fork-and-clone.html>}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-9}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A demonstration repository: \url{https://github.com/data-science-methods/git-demo}
\item
  \emph{Fork step}: Look for the \texttt{fork} button in the upper-right
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/fork-button} 

}

\caption{The *fork* button is near the upper-right corner of a GitHub repository page.  I wasn't able to find a keyboard shortcut for this.  :-(}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-10}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Clone step}: After creating the fork, you need to download a copy to your machine. Open a Terminal window to the folder \emph{right above} where you want the repository folder to be located, eg, \texttt{projects}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
$ git clone https://github.com/YourUsername/git-demo
$ cd git-demo
$ git status
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Push step}: Make changes locally, then push them back up to \emph{your} GitHub copy of the repo
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{lab}{%
\subsection{Lab}\label{lab}}

The lab for this section can be found at \url{https://github.com/data-science-methods/lab-w2-git}.

\hypertarget{r-basics}{%
\section{R Basics}\label{r-basics}}

\hypertarget{lets-get-started-with-rstudio}{%
\subsection{Let's get started with RStudio}\label{lets-get-started-with-rstudio}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Start installing \texttt{tidyverse}
\item
  Quick tour of RStudio's UI
\item
  Let's plot a thing (below)
\item
  Some idiosyncracies of my code

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{\textquotesingle{}} rather than \texttt{"}
  \item
    \texttt{\#\#} for documentation comment
  \item
    \texttt{=} not \texttt{\textless{}-} for assignment
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Essential Global Options

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Never save .Rdata, load workspace
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/Rstudio_config} 

}

\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Super useful keyboard commands:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Insert a pipe: ⌘+Shift+m
  \item
    Comment/uncomment: ⌘+Shift+c
  \item
    Tools \(\to\) Modify Keyboard Shortcuts\ldots{}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      ⌘+1, ⌘+2, ⌘+3
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Cheatsheet: \url{https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rstudio/cheatsheets/master/rstudio-ide.pdf}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{If you're already familiar with the basics of R}, I recommend spending some time with Wickham (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamAdvanced2014}{2014}\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamAdvanced2014}{b}).
\end{itemize}

  Moar plots

\hypertarget{exercise-1}{%

Calculate correlations between each pair of life expectancy, population, and GDP per capita

\hypertarget{base-r-data-types}{%

\hypertarget{factors}{%
\subsubsection{Factors}\label{factors}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{foo\_str =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}foo\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}foo\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}zoo\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{as.integer}\NormalTok{(foo\_str)}
\NormalTok{foo\_fct =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{as.factor}\NormalTok{(foo\_str)}
\KeywordTok{as.integer}\NormalTok{(foo)}
\KeywordTok{levels}\NormalTok{(foo\_fct)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{lists}{%
\subsubsection{Lists}\label{lists}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{some\_list =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}foo\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{some\_list}
\NormalTok{some\_list[}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{]}

\NormalTok{nested\_list =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}foo\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, some\_list)}
\NormalTok{nested\_list}
\NormalTok{nested\_list[[}\DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{]]}

\NormalTok{moar\_list =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{list}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}foo\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{12}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{13}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{moar\_list}\OperatorTok{$}\NormalTok{foo}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{data-frames}{%
\subsubsection{Data frames}\label{data-frames}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{gapminder} is a data frame
\item
  Technical point: a data frame is a list

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    ``Columns'' = list elements
  \item
    Restriction: all of the elements must have the same length
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A tibble (\texttt{tbl\_df}) is a data frame with nice printing (part of the tidyverse)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Plus some technical restrictions that make it nicer to work with
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Like 99\% of the time you create a data frame by loading data or manipulating another data frame. But it's good to know how to create them by hand:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{foo =}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{:}\DecValTok{5}
\NormalTok{bar =}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{letters[}\DecValTok{1}\OperatorTok{:}\DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{]}

\KeywordTok{data.frame}\NormalTok{(foo, bar)}
\KeywordTok{tibble}\NormalTok{(foo, bar)}

\CommentTok{\#\# tribble() is super useful when you\textquotesingle{}re creating a dataframe entirely by hand}
\KeywordTok{tribble}\NormalTok{(}\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{foo, }\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{bar, }
        \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}a\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
        \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}b\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
        \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}c\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
        \DecValTok{4}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}d\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
        \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}e\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{logical-operators}{%
\subsection{Logical operators}\label{logical-operators}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\OtherTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}it}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}\textquotesingle{}}\StringTok{s true!\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\} }\ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}it}\CharTok{\textbackslash{}\textquotesingle{}}\StringTok{s false!\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\NormalTok{foo =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{{-}1}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{{-}3}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{foo }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}
\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (foo }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}greater than zero!\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{any}\NormalTok{(foo }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}at least one is greater than zero!\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}
\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{all}\NormalTok{(foo }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}all are greater than zero!\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\} }\ControlFlowTok{else}\NormalTok{ \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}at least one is not greater than zero!\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (foo }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0} \OperatorTok{|}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{foo }\OperatorTok{\textless{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}non{-}zero\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}
\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (foo }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0} \OperatorTok{||}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{foo }\OperatorTok{\textless{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}non{-}zero\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (foo }\OperatorTok{!=}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}non{-}zero\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}
\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\KeywordTok{all}\NormalTok{(foo }\OperatorTok{!=}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)) \{}
  \KeywordTok{print}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}non{-}zero\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\NormalTok{bar =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{17}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{25}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{{-}14}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{ifelse}\NormalTok{(bar }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}positive\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}negative or zero\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{case\_when}\NormalTok{(bar }\OperatorTok{\textgreater{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}  \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}positive\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{          bar }\OperatorTok{==}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0} \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}zero\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{          bar }\OperatorTok{\textless{}}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{0}  \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}negative\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{functional-programming-and-data-science}{%
\section{Functional Programming and Data Science}\label{functional-programming-and-data-science}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Recommended reading: Chambers (\protect\hyperlink{ref-ChambersObjectOrientedProgrammingFunctional2014}{2014}); parts II and III of Wickham (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamAdvanced2014}{2014}\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamAdvanced2014}{b})
\item
  Programming paradigms

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Procedural}: Software is a series of instructions (``procedures''), which the computer carries out in order. Special instructions (if-then, loops) are used to change the order based on inputs or other conditions.

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Examples: FORTRAN, BASIC, Pascal, C
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \emph{Object-oriented}: Software is made up of objects, which have properties (``attributes,'' including other objects) and do things (``methods'').

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Example: A board game consists of 1 or more players, a board, and a deck of cards.

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        Players each have a hand of cards (attribute), can draw cards from the deck into their hand (method, which also changes the deck's attributes), and can play cards to manipulate tokens on the board (method, which also changes the board's attributes).\\
      \item
        The game itself is an object, with the players, board, and deck for its attributes and methods to things up, run turns in sequence, determine the winner.\\
      \item
        Separate objects might be responsible for displaying the current state of the game and getting input from the users.\\
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      Much better than procedural paradigms for \emph{encapsulation}: When a player draws a card from the deck, we just need to know the inputs and outputs for \texttt{Deck.draw()}, not the details of how the deck tracks the order of cards and which ones have already been drawn.
    \item
      Much easier to reason about systems with lots of dynamic, interacting parts.\\
    \item
      Examples (mostly): Python, C++, Java
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \emph{Functional}: Software is made up of functions, which are run sequentially on the inputs

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Example: Raw data are read in, cleaned, and then a regression model is fit. The model coefficients are tested for statistical significance, and a table and plot are written out to disk.\\
    \item
      \textbf{Data analysis as building a series of pipes}
    \item
      Examples (mostly): R, Lisp, Haskell
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    A couple of extra rules for functional programming

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \emph{Immutability}: Once the value is assigned to a variable, that value cannot be changed
    \item
      \emph{No side effects}: Functions don't change anything; they only return a value
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{warnings-errors-and-getting-help}{%
\section{Warnings, Errors, and Getting Help}\label{warnings-errors-and-getting-help}}

\hypertarget{recommended-reading-and-watching}{%
\subsection{Recommended reading and watching}\label{recommended-reading-and-watching}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \url{https://stackoverflow.com/a/5963610/3187973}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \url{https://github.com/jennybc/debugging\#video}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{messages-warnings-and-errors}{%
\subsection{Messages, warnings, and errors}\label{messages-warnings-and-errors}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Message}: Things are fine, but here's some information you should know
\item
  \emph{Warning}: Uhhhh I'm gonna keep going, but maybe this isn't what you want
\item
  \emph{Error}: Nope. I'm stopping here. You need to fix the thing.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{where-to-go-for-help}{%
\subsection{Where to go for help}\label{where-to-go-for-help}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Isolate the problem
\item
  Restart your session: Session \(\to\) Restart R
\item
  Local help: \texttt{?fun}
\item
  StackOverflow: \url{https://stackoverflow.com/questions/tagged/r}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    CRAN \(\to\) BugReports (usually GitHub Issues)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(lubridate)}

\NormalTok{some\_data =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}June 2002\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}May 15, 2007\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}August 2007\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{mdy}\NormalTok{(some\_data)}
\KeywordTok{parse\_date\_time}\NormalTok{(some\_data, }\DataTypeTok{orders =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}mdY\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Let's try SO: \url{https://stackoverflow.com/search?q=\%5BR\%5D+lubridate+month-year}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{parse\_date\_time}\NormalTok{(some\_data, }\DataTypeTok{orders =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}mY\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}mdY\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Here's another one:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{more\_data =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}May 7, 2017\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}May 19, 2017\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}May Fifth, 2017\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{mdy}\NormalTok{(more\_data)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

SO doesn't seem so helpful: \url{https://stackoverflow.com/search?q=\%5BR\%5D+lubridate+written+days}

Let's check the CRAN page for \texttt{lubridate}: \url{https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/lubridate/index.html}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/lubridate_cran} 

}

\caption{Screenshot of `lubridate` on CRAN, highlighting the BugReports field}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-27}
\end{figure}

Trying a couple of searches gives us a promising result: \url{https://github.com/tidyverse/lubridate/issues?q=is\%3Aissue+is\%3Aopen+mdy}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/lubridate_issues} 

}

\caption{Screenshot of `lubridate` issues page, showing a relevant search result}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-28}
\end{figure}

This is a known bug; it looks like they're thinking about doing something about it, but the only workaround is to create an NA: \url{https://github.com/tidyverse/lubridate/issues/685}

\hypertarget{writing-a-reproducible-example-reprex}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Writing a reproducible example: \texttt{reprex}}{Writing a reproducible example: reprex}}\label{writing-a-reproducible-example-reprex}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \url{https://reprex.tidyverse.org/}
\item
  \url{https://reprex.tidyverse.org/articles/articles/learn-reprex.html}
\item
  \url{https://reprex.tidyverse.org/articles/reprex-dos-and-donts.html}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{do-not-do-these-things-in-your-reprex}{%
\subsection{Do not do these things in your reprex}\label{do-not-do-these-things-in-your-reprex}}

If you do them, \href{https://www.tidyverse.org/blog/2017/12/workflow-vs-script/}{Jenny Bryan will come to your office and set your computer on fire}.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{setwd(\textquotesingle{}/users/danhicks/projects/catsaregreat/myscript/\textquotesingle{})}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Used to ensure that R is running where your file is
  \item
    Unnecessary if you're opening different projects in different RStudio sessions
  \item
    Will cause irrelevant errors on any other system
  \item
    Aside: use \texttt{file.path()} or \texttt{here::here()} to build paths
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{rm(list=ls())}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Used because people think it clears out the global environment
  \item
    Unnecessary if you're regularly using Session \(\to\) Restart R
  \item
    Also unnecessary at the top of a \texttt{Rmd} file, which is always knit in a new session
  \item
    Doesn't actually clear out the global environment

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      eg, doesn't unload packages or reset \texttt{options()}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Not on Bryan's list, but a bonus thing to not do:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{require(package)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If \texttt{package} is installed, will act just like \texttt{library()}
  \item
    If not, will return \texttt{FALSE}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      which generally means the script will keep going until you get some kind of error about a missing function sometime later
    \item
      probably not the error you wanted help with
    \item
      annoying to debug because I have no idea where the function is supposed to come from
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    If \texttt{library()} can't find the package, it immediately raises an error

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      I can tell right away what package needs to be installed
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{part-exploratory-data-analysis-eda}{%
\part{Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA)}\label{part-exploratory-data-analysis-eda}}

\hypertarget{eda-models}{%
\section{Exploratory Data Analysis}\label{eda-models}}

\begin{quote}
This book is about exploratory data analysis, about looking at data to see what it seems to say. It concentrates on simple arithmetic and easy-to-draw pictures. It regards whatever appearances we have recognized as partial descriptions, and tries to look beneath them for new insights. Its concern is with appearance, not with confirmation. Tukey (\protect\hyperlink{ref-TukeyExploratoryDataAnalysis1977}{1977}) v
\end{quote}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA) is ``an attitude, AND a flexibility, AND some graph paper (or transparencies, or both)'' (Tukey \protect\hyperlink{ref-TukeyWeNeedBoth1980}{1980}) developed by statistician John Tukey
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{exploratory-and-confirmatory-research}{%
\subsection{Exploratory and Confirmatory Research}\label{exploratory-and-confirmatory-research}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Especially in the wake of the replication crisis, one common distinction is between exploratory and confirmatory research (Wagenmakers et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-WagenmakersAgendaPurelyConfirmatory2012}{2012})
\end{itemize}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
Confirmatory\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
Exploratory\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
hypothesis testing\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
hypothesis development\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
specified in advance\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
adaptable\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
algorithmic\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
free, creative\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
avoids inferential errors
(type I, false positives)\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
\emph{makes} errors?\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
\emph{rigorous}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{lacking rigor?}\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.42\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{real science??}\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.29\columnwidth}\raggedright
\textbf{h*cking around with data??}\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{better-models-for-eda-i-risk-management-in-spiral-development}{%
\subsection{Better Models for EDA I: Risk Management in Spiral Development}\label{better-models-for-eda-i-risk-management-in-spiral-development}}



\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{images/03-eda/waterfall} \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{images/03-eda/scientific_method} 

}

\caption{The waterfall model of software development resembles the elementary school model of ``the scientific method,'' with an inflexible, linear sequence of predefined steps. Source: \url{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Waterfall_model}}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-31}
\end{figure}



\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/03-eda/spiral} 

}

\caption{Boehm's spiral model of software development, based on Boehm (\protect\hyperlink{ref-BoehmSpiralModelSoftware1988}{1988}), fig.~2. Source: \url{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiral_model}}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-32}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Through the 1970s, software engineering relied on a ``waterfall model'' of development

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Resembles elementary school picture of ``the scientific method'': linear sequences of steps, specified in advance
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{boehmspiralmodelsoftware1988s-spiral-model}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Boehm (\protect\hyperlink{ref-BoehmSpiralModelSoftware1988}{1988})'s ``spiral model''}{Boehm (1988)'s ``spiral model''}}\label{boehmspiralmodelsoftware1988s-spiral-model}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Critique: The waterfall model required extensive advance planning

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Couldn't be adapted as new problems were identified or requirements changed
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Basic idea: Software development works in an expanding spiral, moving from low-cost, low-risk proof of concept to usable demonstration to full product}
\end{itemize}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Define outputs concurrently, not sequentially
\item
  4 basic activities in each cycle

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\alph{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Identify win conditions for all stakeholders
  \item
    Identify and evaluate alternative approaches
  \item
    \textbf{Identify and resolve risks for each approach}
  \item
    Obtain approval and commitment from all stakeholders
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  \textbf{Risk determines level of effort}
\item
  Risk determines degree of details
\item
  Use anchor point milestones

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumii{\alph{enumii}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Is there a sufficient definition of a technical and management approach to satisfying everyone's win conditions?
  \item
    Is there a sufficient definition of the preferred approach to satisfying everyone's win conditions, and are all significant risks eliminated or mitigated?
  \item
    Is there sufficient preparation of the software, site, users, operators, and maintainers to satisfy everyone's win conditions by launching the system?
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{academic-science-in-terms-of-the-spiral-model}{%
\subsubsection{Academic science in terms of the spiral model}\label{academic-science-in-terms-of-the-spiral-model}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Start small: pilot study, subset of big data
\item
  Stakeholders

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    PI, grad researchers, undergrad researchers
  \item
    Dissertation committee, advisor; tenure committee
  \item
    Journal editors and reviewers, funding agency, hiring committees
  \item
    Community partners? Policymakers? Industry?
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{EDA: Identify and mitigate risks before you fully commit to an approach}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Do these data include the main variables we care about?
  \item
    How about additional covariates, predictors, identifiers we need?
  \item
    Where are the data errors? Can we fix them?\\
  \item
    Where are the missing values?
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{better-models-for-eda-ii-epicycle-of-analysis}{%
\subsection{Better Models for EDA II: Epicycle of Analysis}\label{better-models-for-eda-ii-epicycle-of-analysis}}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/03-eda/epicycle} 

}

\caption{Epicycle of analysis model, @PengArtDataScience2016, 5}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-33}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Peng and Matsui (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Data analysis is organized into 5 activities
  \item
    Each activity involves the same 3-step ``epicycle'' process

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Develop expectations
    \item
      Collect information
    \item
      Compare and revise expectations
    \end{enumerate}
  \item
    Not ``the scientific method''! (Peng and Matsui \protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016}, 4)

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      ``Highly iterative and non-linear''
    \item
      "information is learned at each step, which then informs

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        \textbf{whether (and how) to refine, and redo, the {[}previous{]} step} \ldots, or
      \item
        whether (and how) to proceed to the next step."
      \end{itemize}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}ll@{}}
\caption{Aligning the goals of EDA with steps in the ``epicycle of analysis'' (Peng and Matsui \protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016})}\tabularnewline
\toprule
\begin{minipage}[b]{0.43\columnwidth}\raggedright
Goals of EDA\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[b]{0.24\columnwidth}\raggedright

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline

\endfirsthead

\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.43\columnwidth}\raggedright
Determine if there are
problems with the data\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.24\columnwidth}\raggedright
2. Collecting information\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.43\columnwidth}\raggedright
Determine whether our
question can be answered
with these data\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.24\columnwidth}\raggedright
3. Comparing and revising expectations\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.43\columnwidth}\raggedright
Develop sketch of an
answer\strut
\end{minipage} & \begin{minipage}[t]{0.24\columnwidth}\raggedright
1. Developing expectations\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{better-models-for-eda-iii-developing-phenomena}{%
\subsection{Better Models for EDA III: Developing Phenomena}\label{better-models-for-eda-iii-developing-phenomena}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Bogen and Woodward (\protect\hyperlink{ref-BogenSavingPhenomena1988}{1988})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{data}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      observed
    \item
      not explainable (because of random error and idiosyncracies of data collection)
    \item
      local to a time, place, sample, data collection procedure
    \item
      provide evidence for phenomena
    \item
      example: a spreadsheet of numbers, downloaded from Qualtrics
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \emph{phenomena}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      not observed
    \item
      constructed using techniques for data analysis
    \item
      explainable by theories
    \item
      stable over a broader or narrow range of times, places, samples, procedures
    \item
      provide evidence for theories
    \item
      don't explain anything
    \item
      example: correlation between partisan identification and sharing covid misinformation
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \emph{theories}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      not observed
    \item
      constructed to explain phenomena
    \item
      \emph{might} explain deductively (eg, some parts of physics)

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        or might enable a ``detailed tracing of the causal mechanisms responsible for the facts to be explained'' (324)
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      stable over a broader or narrow range of times, places, samples, procedures
    \item
      example: conservative messaging -\textgreater{} anxiety -\textgreater{} susceptibility to conspiracy theories
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{EDA as developing phenomena}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    starting to mitigate random error and idiosyncracies
  \item
    identifying \emph{local} patterns (``local phenomena'')
  \item
    \textbf{not yet claiming these will be stable and appear elsewhere}
  \item
    not yet worrying (much) about explanations
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{discussion-questions}{%
\subsection{Discussion questions}\label{discussion-questions}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  For each of these models, how well do they fit the way you've been taught to do science? How do they challenge the way you've been taught to do science?
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{two-eda-checklists}{%
\section{Two EDA ``Checklists''}\label{two-eda-checklists}}

\hypertarget{pengartdatascience2016}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Peng and Matsui (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016})}{Peng and Matsui (2016)}}\label{pengartdatascience2016}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Formulate your question
\item
  Read in your data
\item
  Check the packaging
\item
  Look at the top and the bottom of your data
\item
  Check your ``n''s
\item
  Validate with at least one external data source
\item
  Make a plot
\item
  Try the easy solution first
\item
  Follow up (Peng and Matsui \protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016}, 33)
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{huebnersystematicapproachinitial2016}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Huebner, Vach, and le Cessie (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HuebnerSystematicApproachInitial2016}{2016})}{Huebner, Vach, and le Cessie (2016)}}\label{huebnersystematicapproachinitial2016}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Duplicate records need to be eliminated
\item
  Direction of numerical codes for categorical and ordinal variables
\item
  Inconsistencies in date and time stamps
\item
  Presence of bimodal distributions
\item
  Presence of skewed distributions
\item
  Presence of ceilings and floors in continuous variables
\item
  Presence of outliers
\item
  Distribution of missing data
\item
  Indications of recording errors in the main variables (after Huebner, Vach, and le Cessie \protect\hyperlink{ref-HuebnerSystematicApproachInitial2016}{2016}, 26)
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{inspecting-variables}{%
\section{Inspecting Variables}\label{inspecting-variables}}

For this EDA, we'll work with data on police stops in Oakland, California, that have been pre-cleaned and released by the Stanford Open Policing Project. Because this analysis focuses on categorical data and counts of observations, most of the elements in Huebner, Vach, and le Cessie (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HuebnerSystematicApproachInitial2016}{2016}) don't really fit. So we'll follow the checklist from Peng and Matsui (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016}).

We'll also be learning how to use three R packages: \texttt{skimr} and \texttt{visdat} for getting an overview of the data in a dataframe, and \texttt{dplyr} for manipulating dataframes. A good cheatsheet for \texttt{dplyr} is available here: \url{https://raw.githubusercontent.com/rstudio/cheatsheets/master/data-transformation.pdf}.

\hypertarget{packages}{%
\subsection{Packages}\label{packages}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(lubridate)   }\CommentTok{\# for working with datetime data}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(vroom)       }\CommentTok{\# for quickly reading large CSV files}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(skimr)       }\CommentTok{\# generate a text{-}based overview of the data}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(visdat)      }\CommentTok{\# generate plots visualizing data types and missingness}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(tictoc)      }\CommentTok{\# for timing how long R takes to do the thing}

\CommentTok{\#\# Set for the HTML output}
\CommentTok{\#\# In RStudio, this is overriden by the width of the console pane}
\KeywordTok{options}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{width =} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)  }\CommentTok{\# wider output for skimr}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{get-the-data}{%
\subsection{Get the Data}\label{get-the-data}}

We'll be using data on police stops in Oakland, California, collected and published by the Stanford Open Policing Project, \url{https://openpolicing.stanford.edu/}. We'll start by making a \texttt{data} folder and downloading the data, if necessary. The URL for the data file can be found on \url{https://openpolicing.stanford.edu/data/}. It will also be useful to have the readme handy, \url{https://github.com/stanford-policylab/opp/blob/master/data_readme.md}.

Because the next chunk of code might be intimidating to type out, and you need the url, I encourage you to copy and paste.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{data\_dir =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}data\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{target\_file =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(data\_dir, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}oakland.zip\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}

\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{dir.exists}\NormalTok{(data\_dir)) \{}
    \KeywordTok{dir.create}\NormalTok{(data\_dir)}
\NormalTok{\}}
\ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{file.exists}\NormalTok{(target\_file)) \{}
    \KeywordTok{download.file}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}https://stacks.stanford.edu/file/druid:yg821jf8611/yg821jf8611\_ca\_oakland\_2020\_04\_01.csv.zip\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{                  target\_file)}
\NormalTok{\}}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{formulate-your-question}{%
\subsection{1. Formulate your question}\label{formulate-your-question}}

The Black Lives Matter protests over the last several years have made us aware of the racial aspects of policing. So we're interested in

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Whether Black people in Oakland might be more likely to be stopped than White people
\item
  Whether Black people who are stopped might be more likely to have contraband
\end{enumerate}

These rough questions will be enough to get us started.

\hypertarget{read-in-your-data}{%
\subsection{2. Read in your data}\label{read-in-your-data}}

The dataset is a zipped \texttt{csv} or comma-separated value file. CSVs are structured like Excel spreadsheets, but are stored in plain text rather than Excel's format.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Not run}
\CommentTok{\#\# The base R function \textasciigrave{}read.csv()\textasciigrave{} can\textquotesingle{}t read a zipped file, and returns gibberish}
\KeywordTok{read.csv}\NormalTok{(target\_file)}

\CommentTok{\#\# The \textasciigrave{}read\_csv()\textasciigrave{} function, part of the tidyverse, takes 1.7 seconds on my laptop, but misinterprets several columns as logicals, resulting in parsing problems.}
\CommentTok{\#\# (What goes wrong?  By default, \textasciigrave{}read\_csv()\textasciigrave{} looks at the first 1,000 rows to guess what the type of each column should be.  As we\textquotesingle{}ll see below, columns like \textasciigrave{}beat\textasciigrave{} and \textasciigrave{}subject\_age\textasciigrave{} are missing on the first 5,000+ rows.  So \textasciigrave{}read\_csv()\textasciigrave{} sees 1,000 missing values and guesses that these columns are \textasciigrave{}logical\textasciigrave{}, with values \textasciigrave{}TRUE\textasciigrave{} or \textasciigrave{}FALSE\textasciigrave{}.  We could fix this by changing the \textasciigrave{}guess\_max\textasciigrave{} argument, but that would slow things down even more.)}
\KeywordTok{tic}\NormalTok{()}
\KeywordTok{read\_csv}\NormalTok{(target\_file)}
\KeywordTok{toc}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# The vroom package is 10x faster on my laptop and doesn\textquotesingle{}t have the parsing problems}
\KeywordTok{tic}\NormalTok{()}
\NormalTok{dataf =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{vroom}\NormalTok{(target\_file)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{check-the-packaging}{%
\subsection{3. Check the packaging}\label{check-the-packaging}}

Peng and Matsui (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016}) use some base R functions to look at dimensions of the dataframe and column (variable) types. \texttt{skimr} is more powerful.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# May take a couple of seconds}
\KeywordTok{skim}\NormalTok{(dataf)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
