##   skim_variable n_missing complete_rate min    max        median n_unique
## 1 time                  2          1.00 0 secs 86340 secs 16:12      1439
## 
## -- Variable type: logical --------------------------------------------------------------------------
##   skim_variable      n_missing complete_rate   mean count                  
## 1 arrest_made                0         1     0.121  FAL: 117217, TRU: 16190
## 2 citation_issued            0         1     0.394  FAL: 80836, TRU: 52571 
## 3 warning_issued             0         1     0.231  FAL: 102545, TRU: 30862
## 4 contraband_found       92250         0.309 0.149  FAL: 35005, TRU: 6152  
## 5 contraband_drugs       92250         0.309 0.0844 FAL: 37684, TRU: 3473  
## 6 contraband_weapons     92250         0.309 0.0299 FAL: 39928, TRU: 1229  
## 7 search_conducted           0         1     0.309  FAL: 92250, TRU: 41157 
## 
## -- Variable type: numeric --------------------------------------------------------------------------
##   skim_variable n_missing complete_rate   mean      sd     p0    p25    p50    p75   p100 hist 
## 1 lat                 114         0.999   37.8  0.0284   37.4   37.8   37.8   37.8   38.1 ▁▁▇▁▁
## 2 lng                 114         0.999 -122.   0.0432 -122.  -122.  -122.  -122.  -119.  ▇▁▁▁▁
## 3 subject_age      102724         0.230   33.2 13.3      10     23     29     41     97   ▇▆▃▁▁
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  133k rows (observations); 28 columns (variables)
\item
  16 variables are handled as characters

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{raw\_row\_number} has 1 unique value per row

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      So it's probably some kind of identifier
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \texttt{subject\_race} and \texttt{subject\_sex} have just 5 and 2 unique values

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      These are probably categorical variables represented as characters
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Similarly with \texttt{type}, \texttt{outcome}, and \texttt{search\_basis}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Though these have lots of missing values (high \texttt{n\_missing}, low \texttt{complete\_rate})
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  1 variable represents the date, and another is \texttt{difftime}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{?difftime} tells us that \texttt{difftime} is used to represent intervals or ``time differences''
  \end{itemize}
\item
  7 logical variables

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A lot of these look like coded outcomes that we might be interested in, eg, \texttt{search\_conducted} and \texttt{contraband\_found}
  \item
    \texttt{search\_conducted} has no missing values, but \texttt{contraband\_found} has a lot of missing values
  \end{itemize}
\item
  For our motivating questions

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Good: \texttt{subject\_race} is 100\% complete
  \item
    Also good: \texttt{search\_conducted} is also 100\% complete
  \item
    Potentially worrisome: \texttt{contraband\_found} is only 31\% complete
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

Let's use \texttt{vis\_miss()}, from \texttt{visdat}, to visualize missing values and check out what's up with \texttt{contraband\_found}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# This raises a warning about large data}
\CommentTok{\# vis\_miss(dataf)}
\CommentTok{\#\# So we\textquotesingle{}ll use sample\_n() to draw a subset}
\KeywordTok{set.seed}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2020{-}09{-}10}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{dataf\_smol =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{sample\_n}\NormalTok{(dataf, }\DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{)}

\KeywordTok{vis\_miss}\NormalTok{(dataf\_smol)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-41-1} \end{center}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Arguments in vis\_miss() are useful for picking up patterns in missing values}
\CommentTok{\#\# cluster = TRUE uses hierarchical clustering to order the rows}
\KeywordTok{vis\_miss}\NormalTok{(dataf\_smol, }\DataTypeTok{cluster =} \OtherTok{TRUE}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-41-2} \end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Several variables related to search outcomes are missing together

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{contraband\_found}, \texttt{contraband\_drugs}, \texttt{contraband\_weapons}, \texttt{search\_basis}, \texttt{use\_of\_force\_description}, \texttt{raw\_subject\_typeofsearch}, and \texttt{raw\_subject\_resultofsearch}
  \item
    However, \texttt{search\_conducted} is complete
  \item
    \textbf{Critical question: When a search has been conducted, do we know whether contraband was found?}
  \item
    Semi-translated: when \texttt{search\_conducted\ ==\ TRUE}, is \texttt{!is.na(contraband\_found)}?
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{filter-mutate-count-and-pipes}{%
\subsection{Filter, mutate, count, and pipes}\label{filter-mutate-count-and-pipes}}

If the answer to this \textbf{critical question} is \textbf{no} (for a lot of rows), then we probably can't use it to answer our motivating questions. We'll use 4 tools from the tidyverse to answer this critical question.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{filter(dataf,\ condition1,\ condition2,\ ...)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Returns a subset of \texttt{dataf} where all of the \texttt{condition}s are true
  \item
    We'll use this to construct a subset where \texttt{search\_conducted\ ==\ TRUE}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{mutate(dataf,\ newvar\ =\ f(oldvar))}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Adds the column \texttt{newvar}; it doesn't change \texttt{oldvar}
  \item
    We'll use this to add a variable, \texttt{contraband\_known}, indicating whether \texttt{contraband\_found} is missing.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{count(dataf,\ group1,\ group2,\ ...)}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Organizes \texttt{dataf} into groups based on the unique values of the \texttt{group} variables, then
  \item
    Counts the number of rows in each group
  \item
    We'll use this to count the number of rows in our subset where the value of \texttt{contraband\_found} is known or missing
  \end{itemize}
\item
  The pipe \texttt{\%\textgreater{}\%}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Lets us string together a series of function calls
  \item
    The output of one function gets passed as the first argument to the next function
  \item
    The tidyverse functions are set up to be pipe-friendly: they take a dataframe as the first argument, and output a dataframe
  \item
    We'll use this to put the function calls together into a single analysis pipeline

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      More functional: We won't be changing \texttt{dataf}
    \item
      Less cluttered: We don't need to create intermediate variables for each step
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(search\_conducted) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{contraband\_known =} \OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{is.na}\NormalTok{(contraband\_found)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(search\_conducted, contraband\_known)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 1 x 3
##   search_conducted contraband_known     n
##   <lgl>            <lgl>            <int>
## 1 TRUE             TRUE             41157
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{look-at-the-top-and-the-bottom-of-your-data}{%
\subsection{4. Look at the top and the bottom of your data}\label{look-at-the-top-and-the-bottom-of-your-data}}

With 28 columns, the dataframe is too wide to print in a readable way. We could use the \texttt{select()} function (from the tidyverse) to extract and print a few columns at a time.

Instead we'll use the base R function \texttt{View()} in an interactive session. This shows us an Excel-like spreadsheet presentation of a dataframe.

\texttt{View()} can cause significant problems if you use it with a large dataframe on a slower machine. So we'll use a pipe: first extract the \texttt{head()} or \texttt{tail()} of the dataset, and then \texttt{View()} it. We'll also go ahead and view \texttt{dataf\_smol}, the subset we created for \texttt{visdat} above.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{head}\NormalTok{() }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{View}\NormalTok{()}

\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{tail}\NormalTok{() }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{View}\NormalTok{()}

\KeywordTok{View}\NormalTok{(dataf\_smol)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Some of my observations:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The ID variable \texttt{raw\_row\_number} can't be turned into a numeric value
\item
  \texttt{location} is a mix of addresses and intersections (``Bond St @ 48TH AVE'')

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If we were going to generate a map using this column, geocoding might be tricky
  \item
    Fortunately we also get latitude and longitude columns
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{use\_of\_force\_description} doesn't seem to be a descriptive text field; instead it seems to be mostly missing or ``handcuffed''
\end{itemize}

We can also use \texttt{skimr} to check data quality by looking at the minimum and maximum values. Do these ranges make sense for what we expect the variable to be?

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{skim}\NormalTok{(dataf)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

## -- Variable type: numeric --------------------------------------------------------------------------
##   skim_variable n_missing complete_rate   mean      sd     p0    p25    p50    p75   p100 hist 
## 1 lat                 114         0.999   37.8  0.0284   37.4   37.8   37.8   37.8   38.1 ▁▁▇▁▁
## 2 lng                 114         0.999 -122.   0.0432 -122.  -122.  -122.  -122.  -119.  ▇▁▁▁▁
## 3 subject_age      102724         0.230   33.2 13.3      10     23     29     41     97   ▇▆▃▁▁
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Date range is April 1, 2013 to December 31, 2017

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    If we break things down by year, we should expect 2013 to have fewer cases
  \item
    For some purposes, we might need to exclude 2013 data: \texttt{filter(dataf,\ date\ \textgreater{}=\ \textquotesingle{}2014-01-01\textquotesingle{})}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Age range is from 10 years old (!) to 97 (!)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Median (\texttt{p50}) is 29; 50\% of values are between 23 and 41
  \item
    For some purposes, we might need to restrict the analysis to working-age adults: \texttt{filter(dataf,\ subject\_age\ \textgreater{}=\ 18,\ subject\_age\ \textless{}\ 65)}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{check-your-ns-and-6.-validate-with-at-least-one-external-data-source}{%
\subsection{5. Check your ``n''s (and) 6. Validate with at least one external data source}\label{check-your-ns-and-6.-validate-with-at-least-one-external-data-source}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Peng and Matsui (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PengArtDataScience2016}{2016}) use an air quality example with a regular sampling rate,

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    so they can calculate exactly how many observations they should have.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  We can't do that here

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    So we'll combine steps 5 and 6 together
  \end{itemize}
\item
  A web search leads us to this City of Oakland page on police stop data: \url{https://www.oaklandca.gov/resources/stop-data}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    The page mentions a Stanford study that was released in June 2016
  \item
    Recall we got our data from the Stanford Open Policing Project
  \item
    Our data run through December 2017
  \item
    So there's a good chance we're using a superset of the ``Stanford study'' data
  \item
    The page links to this report: \url{https://cao-94612.s3.amazonaws.com/documents/OPD-Racial-Impact-Report-2016-2018-Final-16Apr19.pdf}
  \item
    Page 8 has two summary tables that we can compare to our data
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/03-eda/Oakland_report_tables} 

}

\caption{Screenshot of the two summary tables from the Oakland report.  Source: <https://cao-94612.s3.amazonaws.com/documents/OPD-Racial-Impact-Report-2016-2018-Final-16Apr19.pdf>, page 8}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-45}
\end{figure}

\hypertarget{from-dates-to-years}{%
\subsection{From dates to years}\label{from-dates-to-years}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Our data has the particular date of each stop

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    We need to extract the year of each stop

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{lubridate::year()} does exactly this
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Filter to the years in our data that overlap with the tables
  \item
    And then aggregate by year (and gender) using \texttt{count}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{year =} \KeywordTok{year}\NormalTok{(date)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(year }\OperatorTok{\%in\%}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2016}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2017}\NormalTok{)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(year)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 2 x 2
##    year     n
##   <dbl> <int>
## 1  2016 30268
## 2  2017 30715
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{year =} \KeywordTok{year}\NormalTok{(date)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(year }\OperatorTok{\%in\%}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{2016}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{2017}\NormalTok{)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(year, subject\_sex)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 6 x 3
##    year subject_sex     n
##   <dbl> <chr>       <int>
## 1  2016 female       7677
## 2  2016 male        22563
## 3  2016 <NA>           28
## 4  2017 female       7879
## 5  2017 male        22818
## 6  2017 <NA>           18
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{For both years, we have fewer observations than the report table indicates}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Could our data have been pre-filtered?
  \item
    Let's check the documentation for our data: \url{https://github.com/stanford-policylab/opp/blob/master/data_readme.md\#oakland-ca}
  \item
    ``Data is deduplicated on raw columns contactdate, contacttime, streetname, subject\_sdrace, subject\_sex, and subject\_age, reducing the number of records by \textasciitilde5.2\%''

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      The difference with the report is on this order of magnitude,
    \item
      But varies within groups by several percentage points
    \item
      So deduplication \emph{might} explain the difference
    \item
      But in a more serious analysis we might want to check, eg, with the Stanford Open Policing folks
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\hypertarget{make-a-plot}{%
\subsection{7. Make a plot}\label{make-a-plot}}

Plotting is a whole additional discussion. We'll talk about it next week.

\hypertarget{try-the-easy-solution-first}{%
\subsection{8. Try the easy solution first}\label{try-the-easy-solution-first}}

We are interested in two rough questions:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Whether Black people in Oakland might be more likely to be stopped than White people
\item
  Whether Black people who are stopped might be more likely to have contraband
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The easy solution is to count rows, mostly using the tools we've already developed.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    We'll use \texttt{mutate()} after \texttt{count()} to calculate shares, ie, what fraction of the total are in each group
  \item
    We'll also introduce the function \texttt{arrange()}, to sort the dataframe from greatest to smallest share
  \item
    And \texttt{group\_by()} to calculate shares within racial groups
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{number-of-stops-by-race}{%
\subsubsection{Number of stops, by race}\label{number-of-stops-by-race}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(subject\_race) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{share =}\NormalTok{ n }\OperatorTok{/}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{sum}\NormalTok{(n)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{arrange}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{desc}\NormalTok{(share))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 5 x 3
##   subject_race               n  share
##   <chr>                  <int>  <dbl>
## 1 black                  78925 0.592 
## 2 hispanic               26257 0.197 
## 3 white                  15628 0.117 
## 4 asian/pacific islander  8099 0.0607
## 5 other                   4498 0.0337
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Police stop demographics

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    60\% of subjects stopped by police are Black
  \item
    19\% are Hispanic
  \item
    12\% are White
  \item
    6\% are API
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Oakland demographics: \url{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oakland,_California\#Race_and_ethnicity}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    23\% of residents are Black
  \item
    27\% are Hispanic
  \item
    29\% are non-Hispanic White
  \item
    15\% are Asian
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Comparing the two

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \emph{Blacks are severely overrepresented in police stops}
  \item
    Hispanics and API folks are slightly underrepresented
  \item
    Whites are significantly underrepresented
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{contraband-search-results-by-race}{%
\subsubsection{Contraband search results, by race}\label{contraband-search-results-by-race}}

As a preliminary question, what fraction of stops had a search? Are there disparities by race there? We can answer the first question using tools we already have.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# What fraction of stops had a search? }
\CommentTok{\#\# 31\%}
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(search\_conducted) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{share =}\NormalTok{ n }\OperatorTok{/}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{sum}\NormalTok{(n))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 2 x 3
##   search_conducted     n share
##   <lgl>            <int> <dbl>
## 1 FALSE            92250 0.691
## 2 TRUE             41157 0.309
\end{verbatim}

Across all subjects, 31\% of stops involved a search.

For the other question, we need to calculate something like \texttt{share} \emph{within} racial groups. That is, among all stops \emph{of Black folks}, what fraction involved a search? The \texttt{group\_by()} function divides the dataset into groups that other tidyverse functions respect. Specifically, \texttt{count()} and \texttt{mutate()} will both analyze each group defined by \texttt{subject\_race} separately.

After using \texttt{group\_by()}, it's good practice to always call \texttt{ungroup()}. We don't want to accidentally work with the grouped version in another part of our analysis.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# What fraction of stops had a search, by race? }
\CommentTok{\#\# Note that, for each racial group, \textasciigrave{}share\textasciigrave{} adds up to 100\%. }
\CommentTok{\#\# }
\CommentTok{\#\# For all groups, most stops didn\textquotesingle{}t involve a search; }
\CommentTok{\#\# For Black subjects, 38\% of stops involved a search; }
\CommentTok{\#\# For White subjects, 15\% of stops involved a search. }
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{group\_by}\NormalTok{(subject\_race) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(search\_conducted) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{share =}\NormalTok{ n }\OperatorTok{/}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{sum}\NormalTok{(n)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{ungroup}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 10 x 4
##    subject_race           search_conducted     n share
##    <chr>                  <lgl>            <int> <dbl>
##  1 asian/pacific islander FALSE             6795 0.839
##  2 asian/pacific islander TRUE              1304 0.161
##  3 black                  FALSE            48900 0.620
##  4 black                  TRUE             30025 0.380
##  5 hispanic               FALSE            19535 0.744
##  6 hispanic               TRUE              6722 0.256
##  7 other                  FALSE             3792 0.843
##  8 other                  TRUE               706 0.157
##  9 white                  FALSE            13228 0.846
## 10 white                  TRUE              2400 0.154
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  For all groups, most stops didn't involve a search
\item
  For Black subjects, 38\% of stops involved a search
\item
  For White subjects, 15\% of stops involved a search
\item
  \emph{So police were much more likely to search stopped Black subjects than White subjects}
\end{itemize}

Finally, let's consider our second main question: contraband results from searches. Note that we want to restrict ourselves to only stops where \texttt{search\_conducted} is true.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# Contraband results from searches, by race}
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(search\_conducted) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(subject\_race, contraband\_found) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{group\_by}\NormalTok{(subject\_race) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{share =}\NormalTok{ n }\OperatorTok{/}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{sum}\NormalTok{(n)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{ungroup}\NormalTok{() }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(contraband\_found)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 5 x 4
##   subject_race           contraband_found     n share
##   <chr>                  <lgl>            <int> <dbl>
## 1 asian/pacific islander TRUE               176 0.135
## 2 black                  TRUE              4364 0.145
## 3 hispanic               TRUE              1116 0.166
## 4 other                  TRUE                85 0.120
## 5 white                  TRUE               411 0.171
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  For Black subjects who were searched, contraband was found 15\% of the time
\item
  For White subjects, 17\% of the time
\end{itemize}

\textbf{This preliminary analysis indicates that Black subjects were more likely to be searched than White subjects; but, when they were searched, White subjects were more likely to have contraband.}

\hypertarget{follow-up}{%
\subsection{9. Follow up}\label{follow-up}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  There are several directions we could take this analysis:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Investigate outstanding questions about quality and reliability of the data

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      eg, follow up with Stanford Open Policing Project about the difference in row counts
    \item
      Fits with \emph{risk management} (spiral model): spend a little more time checking to make sure the data aren't corrupted
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Break down our question into more fine-grained analyses

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      eg, the Oakland web site and report talk about policy changes; do we see changes by year in the data?
    \item
      Fits with \emph{epicycle of analysis}: refine and specify research questions
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Apply more sophisticated statistical analysis

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      eg, a regression model to control for age, gender, and other covariates
    \item
      Fits with \emph{phenomena development}: reducing data, eliminating noise, in order to identity local phenomena
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{discussion-questions-1}{%
\subsection{Discussion questions}\label{discussion-questions-1}}

Time permitting, we'll discuss these during class. But more likely we'll talk about them via Slack.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Suppose you've conducted this EDA because you're working with an activist organization that promotes defunding the police and prison abolition. Should you share the preliminary findings above with your organization contacts?
\item
  What influence should the following factors make to your answer?

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Funding: Whether you're being paid as a consultant vs.~volunteering your expertise
  \item
    Values: Your own views about policing and prisons
  \item
    Relationships: Whether you are friends with members of the activist organization and/or police
  \item
    Communications: The degree to which you can control whether and how the organization will publish your preliminary findings
  \item
    Timeliness: Whether these findings are relevant to a pending law or policy change
  \end{itemize}
\item
  What other factors should be taken into account as you decide whether to share your findings? Or not taken into account?
\item
  How has this ``raw data'' been shaped by upstream decisions? (Think of Leonelli's relational account of data.)
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{lab-1}{%
\subsection{Lab}\label{lab-1}}

The lab related to this material is available at \url{https://github.com/data-science-methods/lab-w04-eda}.

\hypertarget{joins}{%
\section{Joins}\label{joins}}

\emph{Joins} are an extremely powerful tool for data science, allowing us to combine two datasets by matching columns.

Some common scenarios:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Data from two different sources for the same units

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Pre- and post-treatment
  \item
    Multi-round experiments / survey panels
  \item
    Advertising and vote share
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Data on different levels

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Students in classrooms in schools
  \item
    Census block groups in counties in states
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{types-of-joins}{%
\subsection{Types of joins}\label{types-of-joins}}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{images/03-eda/join-rows} \includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{images/03-eda/join-venn} 

}

\caption{Join operations combine two dataframes based on one or more matching columns.  Different join operations keep different rows from the combination.  Sources: <https://data-lessons.github.io/gapminder-R/>; <https://notchained.hatenablog.com/entry/2015/01/03/160700>}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-52}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Suppose we have two dataframes, \texttt{left} and \texttt{right}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(tidyverse)}

\NormalTok{left =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{tribble}\NormalTok{(}
    \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{animal, }\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{zoo,}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}aardvark\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Sacramento\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}aardvark\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}San Diego\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}chimpanzee\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}San Diego\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}zebra\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}San Diego\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{right =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{tribble}\NormalTok{(}
    \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{animal, }\OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\NormalTok{food, }
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}aardvark\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}insects\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}chimpanzee\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}insects\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}chimpanzee\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}fruit\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
    \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}velociraptor\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}animals\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Left join}: keep all of the rows and columns from \texttt{left}; add columns from \texttt{right}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{left\_join}\NormalTok{(left, right, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}animal\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 5 x 3
##   animal     zoo        food   
##   <chr>      <chr>      <chr>  
## 1 aardvark   Sacramento insects
## 2 aardvark   San Diego  insects
## 3 chimpanzee San Diego  insects
## 4 chimpanzee San Diego  fruit  
## 5 zebra      San Diego  <NA>
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Right join}: keep all of the rows and columns from \texttt{right}; add columns from \texttt{left}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{right\_join}\NormalTok{(left, right, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}animal\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 5 x 3
##   animal       zoo        food   
##   <chr>        <chr>      <chr>  
## 1 aardvark     Sacramento insects
## 2 aardvark     San Diego  insects
## 3 chimpanzee   San Diego  insects
## 4 chimpanzee   San Diego  fruit  
## 5 velociraptor <NA>       animals
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Full join}: keep all rows and columns from both \texttt{left} and \texttt{right}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{full\_join}\NormalTok{(left, right, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}animal\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 6 x 3
##   animal       zoo        food   
##   <chr>        <chr>      <chr>  
## 1 aardvark     Sacramento insects
## 2 aardvark     San Diego  insects
## 3 chimpanzee   San Diego  insects
## 4 chimpanzee   San Diego  fruit  
## 5 zebra        San Diego  <NA>   
## 6 velociraptor <NA>       animals
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Inner join}: keep all columns, but only rows that match
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{inner\_join}\NormalTok{(left, right, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}animal\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 4 x 3
##   animal     zoo        food   
##   <chr>      <chr>      <chr>  
## 1 aardvark   Sacramento insects
## 2 aardvark   San Diego  insects
## 3 chimpanzee San Diego  insects
## 4 chimpanzee San Diego  fruit
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Anti-join}: keep all columns from \texttt{left}, but only the rows that \emph{don't} match
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{anti\_join}\NormalTok{(left, right, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}animal\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 1 x 2
##   animal zoo      
##   <chr>  <chr>    
## 1 zebra  San Diego
\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{hazards}{%
\subsection{Hazards}\label{hazards}}

Joins can create a number of hazards.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Missing values} from left, right, and full joins

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    In a left join, rows from \texttt{left} that don't match will have missing values in the columns from \texttt{right}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Row counts} can be difficult to predict in advance

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Rows will be copied if they match multiple times
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Column names} can be changed if they're repeated across dataframes but not used for matching
\end{itemize}

To manage these hazards

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Use \texttt{anti\_join()} to figure out where missing values will be
\item
  Use \texttt{select()} with \texttt{distinct()} to check for unexpected duplications
\item
  Use \texttt{names()} to check column names
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{data}{%
\subsubsection{Data}\label{data}}

To examine these hazards, we'll use the \texttt{nycflights13} dataset, which includes all flights that departed New York City in 2013.

The dataset comprises 5 different tables, which of which can be linked to the others using joins. We'll focus on combining \texttt{flights} with \texttt{airports} and \texttt{weather}.

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/03-eda/relational-nycflights} 

}

\end{figure}

\hypertarget{a-good-join}{%
\subsubsection{A good join}\label{a-good-join}}

\emph{Expectation}: For every flight, we can match the origin code to \texttt{airports}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{goodjoin =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{left\_join}\NormalTok{(flights, airports, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}origin\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}faa\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}

\KeywordTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(flights)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 336776
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{nrow}\NormalTok{(goodjoin)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] 336776
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# If we couldn\textquotesingle{}t match an origin code, \textasciigrave{}tzone\textasciigrave{} would be NA}
\NormalTok{goodjoin }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{is.na}\NormalTok{(tzone))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

\end{verbatim}

Manage this using \texttt{anti\_join()} to determine which airports aren't in \texttt{origin}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{bad-join-2}{%
\subsubsection{Bad join \#2}\label{bad-join-2}}

Calculate the average daily temperature at each airport.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{avg\_temp =}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{weather }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{group\_by}\NormalTok{(origin, year, month, day) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{summarize}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{temp =} \KeywordTok{mean}\NormalTok{(temp)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{ungroup}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## `summarise()` regrouping output by 'origin', 'year', 'month' (override with `.groups` argument)
\end{verbatim}

\emph{Expectation}: Each flight has 1 average daily temperature at its origin.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

                           \DataTypeTok{by =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}year\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}month\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}day\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}

\end{verbatim}

One way to figure out what happened is to check the names of the columns. The \texttt{dplyr} join functions add \texttt{.x} and \texttt{.y} when they encounter repeated column names.

\hypertarget{visual-eda}{%
\section{Visual EDA}\label{visual-eda}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Recommended reading: Weissgerber et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WeissgerberBarLineGraphs2015}{2015}); Wickham (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamLayeredGrammarGraphics2010}{2010})
\item
  Further reading: Healy (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HealyDataVisualizationPractical2018}{2018}), ch.~1; Matejka and Fitzmaurice (\protect\hyperlink{ref-MatejkaSameStatsDifferent}{n.d.})
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-role-of-visuals-in-eda}{%
\subsection{The Role of Visuals in EDA}\label{the-role-of-visuals-in-eda}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Tukey (\protect\hyperlink{ref-TukeyExploratoryDataAnalysis1977}{1977}) emphasized visual methods for EDA
\item
  Including not just graphs but also structured tables, such as \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stem-and-leaf_display}{stem-and-leaf displays}
\item
  In terms of our 3 models of EDA (ch.~\ref{eda-models})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Risk management: With the right plot, our visual system can very efficiently detect potential problems in the data that don't show up easily with summary statistics

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Outliers
    \item
      Degeneracies (eg, perfect correlation)
    \item
      Skew or bimodal distributions
    \item
      Non-linear relationships
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Epicycle of analysis:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Check expectations about the distribution of variables
    \item
      Develop expectations about relationships between variables
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Phenomena development:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Quickly identify potential patterns
    \item
      Contrast potential patterns with noise/uncertainty/imprecision
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{plot-your-data}{%
\subsection{Plot Your Data}\label{plot-your-data}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Summary statistics almost always focus only on central tendency (mean, median) and dispersion (standard deviation, IQR)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    This is all you would need if the world were made of normal distributions
  \item
    The world is not made of normal distributions (Lyon \protect\hyperlink{ref-LyonWhyAreNormal2014}{2014})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  We'll illustrate this using The Datasaurus Dozen, a collection of 13 datasets (Matejka and Fitzmaurice \protect\hyperlink{ref-MatejkaSameStatsDifferent}{n.d.})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Each dataset has two variables, \texttt{x} and \texttt{y}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(tidyverse)}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(datasauRus)}
\NormalTok{dataf =}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{datasaurus\_dozen}

\NormalTok{dataf}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The datasets have the same means, standard deviations, and (Pearson) correlation coefficient

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    p-value of the correlation coefficient is not statistically significant
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\# This is a more complex summarize() call than we\textquotesingle{}ve seen before}
\CommentTok{\#\# 1. Number of rows}
\CommentTok{\#\# 2. "Summarize across the columns x and y, using the functions mean and sd"; automatically generates names}
\CommentTok{\#\# 3. Correlation between x and y}
\CommentTok{\#\# 4. p{-}value from a t{-}test of the null that the correlation = 0}
\NormalTok{dataf }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{group\_by}\NormalTok{(dataset) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{summarize}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{n =} \KeywordTok{n}\NormalTok{(), }
              \KeywordTok{across}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{.cols =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(x, y), }
                     \DataTypeTok{.fns =} \KeywordTok{lst}\NormalTok{(mean, sd)), }
              \DataTypeTok{cor\_xy =} \KeywordTok{cor}\NormalTok{(x, y), }
              \DataTypeTok{p\_value =} \KeywordTok{cor.test}\NormalTok{(x, y)}\OperatorTok{$}\NormalTok{p.value) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{ungroup}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\end{verbatim}

\hypertarget{the-grammar-of-graphics}{%
\section{The Grammar of Graphics}\label{the-grammar-of-graphics}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Reading: Wilkinson (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WilkinsonGrammarGraphics1999}{1999}); Wickham (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamLayeredGrammarGraphics2010}{2010})
\item
  A lot of programming sublanguages for creating graphics are based on the \emph{artist's palette} model

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Define some low-level tools for drawing on a canvas: \texttt{points()}, \texttt{lines()}, \texttt{curve()}
  \item
    One-liners that bundle these tools into standard plots: \texttt{hist()}, \texttt{barplot()}
  \item
    This is used by base graphics in R, Python packages including \texttt{matplotlib} and \texttt{seaborn}, MATLAB, etc.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Grammar of graphics} is a fundamentally different approach

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Implemented by \texttt{ggplot2} in the tidyverse
  \item
    (Strictly speaking, \texttt{ggplot} is another package that Hadley Wickham created before \texttt{ggplot2}. But almost no one uses the older package. If people talk about ``ggplot,'' they almost always mean \texttt{ggplot2}.)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=1.2\linewidth]{images/03-eda/grammar_of_graphics} 

}

\caption{The Grammar of Graphics regards graphs as mappings from variables in the data to features of geometric objects. This means that every graph is a model.}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-73}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Graphs are maps}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A graph maps variables in the data to features of geometric objects
  \item
    An individual map is called an \emph{aesthetic}
  \item
    So a graph is a collection of aesthetics applied to a dataset
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Graphs are models}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Simplifications of the data
  \item
    Designed to fit with our cognitive capabilities
  \item
    In ways that support pattern detection, inference
  \item
    Recall Leonelli's relational view of data
  \item
    (Cog sci folks: would you use the term ``cognitive affordance'' here?)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{common-and-less-common-graphs-for-eda}{%
\section{Common (and less common) Graphs for EDA}\label{common-and-less-common-graphs-for-eda}}

One of the major advantages of the grammar of graphics --- especially as implemented in the tidyverse package \texttt{ggplot2} --- is that it enables us to rapidly create \emph{and re-create} graphs. This speed and flexibility is extremely useful for EDA.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\# install.packages(c(\textquotesingle{}tidyverse\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}skimr\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}gghighlight\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}ggbeeswarm\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}ggforce\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}ggupset\textquotesingle{}))}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(tidyverse)}

\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(skimr)}

\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggbeeswarm)}
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggforce)}
\CommentTok{\# library(gghighlight)}
\CommentTok{\# library(ggupset)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

While \texttt{ggplot2} is supposed to have ``pretty defaults,'' for a long time its default color scales have been \emph{terrible}, with bad color combinations for color-blind folks, poor rendering in black and white, and low contrast against the default gray background.

The options below should set a more color-blind-friendly default palette and a plain white background.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{options}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{ggplot2.discrete.fill =} \KeywordTok{partial}\NormalTok{(colorspace}\OperatorTok{::}\NormalTok{scale\_fill\_discrete\_qualitative, }\DataTypeTok{palette =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Dark 2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
        \DataTypeTok{ggplot2.discrete.colour =} \KeywordTok{partial}\NormalTok{(colorspace}\OperatorTok{::}\NormalTok{scale\_color\_discrete\_qualitative, }\DataTypeTok{palette =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}Dark 2\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}

\KeywordTok{theme\_set}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{theme\_bw}\NormalTok{())}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{data-1}{%
\subsection{Data}\label{data-1}}

We'll be looking at a collection of US college tuition and diversity data. I acquired the data from \href{https://github.com/rfordatascience/tidytuesday/tree/master/data/2020/2020-03-10}{TidyTuesday (2020-03-10)}, which in turn sourced it from some combination of \href{https://www.tuitiontracker.org/}{Tuition Tracker} and the \emph{Chronicle of Higher Education}.

The documentation in this TidyTuesday repo is quite poor, and doesn't actually say exactly where each dataset came from. Some of the data is parsed from downloaded HTML files; other data is produced by non-reproducible scripts that clean CSV files. My best guess is that the tuition data comes \href{https://www.chronicle.com/article/tuition-and-fees-1998-99-through-2018-19/}{a \emph{Chronicle of Higher Education} table} with AY 2018-19 ``sticker prices'' for tuition. And I think the diversity data comes from \href{https://www.chronicle.com/article/student-diversity-at-4-725-institutions/}{another \emph{CHE} table} which reports counts of all students (not just undergrads) enrolled in Fall 2014.

To facilitate some later steps, I filtered the datasets we'll be using to \emph{4-year colleges and universities} in \emph{California}. I also standardized the names of the Cal State (California State University, or CSU) and University of California campuses, and identified which ``system'' each school is in (CSU, UC, private, or for-profit). The prepared data files (along with the cleaning script and draft versions of the plots below) is available \href{https://github.com/data-science-methods/ca-tuition-diversity}{here}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{data\_dir =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}data\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{data\_files =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(data\_dir, }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}diversity.csv\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}tuition.csv\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}

\NormalTok{data\_urls =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{str\_c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}https://github.com/data{-}science{-}methods/ca{-}tuition{-}diversity/raw/master/\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, data\_files)}

\CommentTok{\#\# \textasciigrave{}if\textasciigrave{} isn\textquotesingle{}t vectorized, so we have to loop over the files/URLs manually}
\NormalTok{download\_file =}\StringTok{ }\ControlFlowTok{function}\NormalTok{(path, url) \{}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{file.exists}\NormalTok{(path)) \{}
        \KeywordTok{download.file}\NormalTok{(url, path)}
\NormalTok{    \}}
\NormalTok{\}}
\NormalTok{purrr}\OperatorTok{::}\KeywordTok{walk2}\NormalTok{(data\_files, data\_urls, download\_file)}

\NormalTok{div\_df =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{read\_csv}\NormalTok{(data\_files[}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

\end{Highlighting}

\begin{verbatim}

##   name = col_character(),

##   out_of_state_total = col_double(),
##   system = col_character()
## )
\end{verbatim}

\texttt{tuition\_df} contains data on tuition (the ``sticker price,'' not what students actually pay). There are 135 rows, 1 row for each of the public, private, and for-profit 4-year schools in California. There are a few different calculations of tuition or cost; we'll only be looking at \texttt{in\_state\_tuition}.
In this dataset, \texttt{type} is either public, private, or for-profit. All of the public 4-year colleges or universities in California belong to either the Cal State or UC system. \texttt{system} distinguishes these two public systems, and also carries over the values from \texttt{type} for private and for-profit schools.

\texttt{div\_df} has 5027 rows, corresponding to unique combinations of school (\texttt{name}) and demographic group (\texttt{category}). \texttt{share} gives the fraction of students enrolled at the school in the given group. Because the TidyTuesday dataset didn't have the \texttt{degree\_length} variable, I wasn't able to filter down to only 4-year schools. That won't be a problem later.

\KeywordTok{count}\NormalTok{(div\_df, category)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 11 x 2
##    category                               n
##    <chr>                              <int>
##  1 American Indian / Alaska Native      457
##  2 Asian                                457
##  3 Black                                457

\hypertarget{d-distributions}{%
\subsection{1D distributions}\label{d-distributions}}

\hypertarget{categorical-barplot}{%
\subsubsection{Categorical: Barplot}\label{categorical-barplot}}

Counts of observations for a single categorical variable. It's a bar geom with a count statistic. If we keep the statistic but change the geom, we get a point version of a barplot.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, }\DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_bar}\NormalTok{()}
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, }\DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_count}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-80-1} \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-80-2} \end{center}

If we keep the statistic but change the geom, we can make a point version of a barplot. Or a version with text labels.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_count}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}point\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{size =} \DecValTok{3}\NormalTok{)}
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_count}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}label\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{label =} \KeywordTok{after\_stat}\NormalTok{(count)))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-81-1} \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-81-2} \end{center}

Because of ggplot's layering system, we're not restricted to just a barplot or one of these variations. We can combine geoms into a single plot.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_count}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_count}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}label\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{label =} \KeywordTok{after\_stat}\NormalTok{(count)), }
               \DataTypeTok{position =} \KeywordTok{position\_nudge}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{y =} \DecValTok{5}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-82-1} \end{center}

\hypertarget{numeric-histograms-and-density-plots}{%
\subsubsection{Numeric: Histograms and density plots}\label{numeric-histograms-and-density-plots}}

A histogram is a count of observations for a single categorical variable. It uses the same geom as the barplot, but a statistic of counts of \emph{binned} values for a continuous variable.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## `stat_bin()` using `bins = 30`. Pick better value with `binwidth`.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_bin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## `stat_bin()` using `bins = 30`. Pick better value with `binwidth`.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.4\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-83-1} \includegraphics[width=0.4\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-83-2} \end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{binwidth} can have a dramatic effect on the appearance of the plot
\item
  \texttt{binwidth} is a free parameter in constructing the histogram as a model of the data
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{binwidth =} \DecValTok{100}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-84-1} \end{center}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{binwidth =} \DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-84-2} \end{center}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{binwidth =} \DecValTok{10000}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-84-3} \end{center}

A frequency polygon uses the same count statistic, but displays the result with a line rather than columns.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_bin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-85-1} \end{center}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_bin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}line\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## `stat_bin()` using `bins = 30`. Pick better value with `binwidth`.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-85-2} \end{center}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_freqpoly}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## `stat_bin()` using `bins = 30`. Pick better value with `binwidth`.
\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-85-3} \end{center}

Density plots are a smoothed version of histograms.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Often drawn with a rug, which indicates the locations of observed values on the x-axis
\item
  Like histogram, is a model of the data
\item
  Unlike histogram, people often overlook the free parameters (see \texttt{?density})
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_density}\NormalTok{() }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_rug}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-86-1} \end{center}

A strip plot simply plots the values on a single axis.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Unlike all of the other examples here, it doesn't involve any free parameters or other modeling decisions.\\
\item
  But it's also not very useful
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_point}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{y =} \DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{ylim}\NormalTok{(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{.}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{, }\FloatTok{.1}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-87-1} \end{center}

\hypertarget{d-distributions-categorical-x-numeric}{%
\subsection{2D distributions: Categorical x Numeric}\label{d-distributions-categorical-x-numeric}}

When there are only a few values of a categorical variable, it can be useful to simply plot points for individual values.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{fct\_reorder}\NormalTok{(name, in\_state\_tuition),}
\NormalTok{                       in\_state\_tuition, }
                       \DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_point}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{coord\_flip}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-88-1} \end{center}

But we probably don't care about the names of individual schools. One option is a stacked histogram, using color (fill) to indicate school system.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{binwidth =} \DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-89-1} \end{center}

These can be difficult to read when it's not clear whether the bars are stacked on top of each other or layered, one behind the other. Here the bars are layered. The differences are very subtle but potentially important.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_histogram}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{binwidth =} \DecValTok{1000}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{position =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}identity\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-90-1} \end{center}

So it's more common to put the categorical variable on the x-axis. Here's a traditional boxplot. While color is strictly unnecessary, using color for the same categorical variable across plots helps us track relationships. Notice that we can change the way color is used by simply changing which aesthetic gets \texttt{system}.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_boxplot}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_boxplot}\NormalTok{()}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

However, bowplots have many of the problems that Weissgerber et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WeissgerberBarLineGraphs2015}{2015}) identify with bar-and-whisker plots. Neither is actually very good at showing non-gaussian distributions of the underlying data.

A violin plot is better for this. The violin plot calculates densities across the numerical variable (tuition) within groups defined by the categorical variable (system), and shows them symmetrically. We can use the \texttt{draw\_quantiles} argument to show the medians, and \texttt{scale\ =\ \textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}} to get them to have the same width (rather than the same area, the default).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_violin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{draw\_quantiles =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{scale =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-92-1} \end{center}

We can add points to this, using \texttt{position\ =\ \textquotesingle{}jitter\textquotesingle{}} to reduce overplotting and \texttt{alpha\ =\ .5} to make them 50\% transparent.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_violin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{draw\_quantiles =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{scale =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_point}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{position =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}jitter\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{alpha =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-93-1} \end{center}

\texttt{ggforce::geom\_sina()} tries to match the amount of jitter to the width of the violin plot, using the same \texttt{scale} argument. It's a little better, because now the points aren't outside of the violin. But it's still hard to see a visual pattern.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_violin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{draw\_quantiles =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{scale =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_sina}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{scale =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-94-1} \end{center}

A beeswarm plot positions the points so that they cluster close together in the x-direction without too much overplotting. This allows us to show both the individual points and the density distribution, with roughly the same shape (after tweaking some parameters).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_violin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{draw\_quantiles =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{scale =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_beeswarm}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{cex =} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-95-1} \end{center}

Using ggplot it is possible to create the bar-and-whiskers plot that Weissgerber et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WeissgerberBarLineGraphs2015}{2015}) criticize. But now we have much better options.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(tuition\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, in\_state\_tuition, }
                       \DataTypeTok{fill =}\NormalTok{ system, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_summary}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}bar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{stat\_summary}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{geom =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}errorbar\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{width =} \FloatTok{.2}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## No summary function supplied, defaulting to `mean_se()`
## No summary function supplied, defaulting to `mean_se()`
\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-96-1} \end{center}

\hypertarget{joining-tuition-and-diversity-data}{%
\subsection{Joining tuition and diversity data}\label{joining-tuition-and-diversity-data}}

The other numeric variables in the tuition data are variations on in-state tuition: they add room and board (\texttt{in\_state\_total}) or tuition for out-of-state tuitions (\texttt{out\_of\_state\_tuition}), or both. For something more interesting, we'll join the tuition data to the diversity data.

Recall that \texttt{div\_df} has one row for each \emph{combination} of school and demographic group (\texttt{category}).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{div\_df}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## # A tibble: 5,027 x 6
##    name               total_enrollment state     category                         enrollment   share
##    <chr>                         <dbl> <chr>     <chr>                                 <dbl>   <dbl>
##  1 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Women                                 36116 0.705  
##  2 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ American Indian / Alaska Native         482 0.00941
##  3 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Asian                                   645 0.0126 
##  4 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Black                                 18727 0.365  
##  5 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Hispanic                               4792 0.0935 
##  6 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Native Hawaiian / Pacific Islan~        405 0.00790
##  7 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ White                                 23596 0.461  
##  8 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Two Or More Races                      1539 0.0300 
##  9 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Unknown                                 955 0.0186 
## 10 Ashford University            51237 Californ~ Non-Resident Foreign                     96 0.00187
## # ... with 5,017 more rows
\end{verbatim}

First we'll do some checks for the hazards discussed in the ``Joins'' chapter.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{pull}\NormalTok{(name)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]

\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
##  [1] "name"                 "state.x"              "state_code"           "type"                
##  [5] "degree_length"        "room_and_board"       "in_state_tuition"     "in_state_total"      
##  [9] "out_of_state_tuition" "out_of_state_total"   "system"               "total_enrollment"    
## [13] "state.y"              "category"             "enrollment"           "share"
\end{verbatim}

Now we'll actually do the join.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{comb\_df =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{inner\_join}\NormalTok{(div\_df, tuition\_df, }\DataTypeTok{by =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}name\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}state\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{school-diversity-violin-plots}{%
\subsection{School Diversity Violin Plots}\label{school-diversity-violin-plots}}

Because of the way \texttt{div\_df} is structured, it makes sense to separate all of the plots for it into separate panels using the \texttt{category} variable. We've used \texttt{facet\_wrap()} in this way before.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(comb\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(system, share, }\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_violin}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{scale =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}width\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{,}
                \DataTypeTok{draw\_quantiles =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_beeswarm}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{alpha =} \FloatTok{.25}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{cex =} \DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{facet\_wrap}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{vars}\NormalTok{(category), }\DataTypeTok{scales =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}free\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## Warning in regularize.values(x, y, ties, missing(ties), na.rm = na.rm): collapsing to unique 'x'
## values

## Warning in regularize.values(x, y, ties, missing(ties), na.rm = na.rm): collapsing to unique 'x'
## values

## Warning in regularize.values(x, y, ties, missing(ties), na.rm = na.rm): collapsing to unique 'x'
## values
\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-100-1} \end{center}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  No school in the data has more than 5\% American Indian/Alaska Native students.\\
\item
  The numbers are even lower for Native Hawaiian/Pacific Islanders.
\item
  Just a few schools have more than 10\% Black students.\\
\item
  The median UC and CSU schools have greater ``Total Minority'' students than the private schools.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    But there's a lot of variation within the CSU system and across private schools.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  UC and CSU schools have different groups of minority students

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    UCs have a higher share of Asian students than Cal States
  \item
    Cal States have a higher share of Hispanic students than UCs
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{d-distributions-numeric-x-numeric}{%
\subsection{2D Distributions: Numeric x Numeric}\label{d-distributions-numeric-x-numeric}}

A point geom gives us a basic scatterplot. \texttt{geom\_smooth()} adds a predictive model. By default, this predictive model is either a LOESS (also called local regression; for smaller datasets) or a generalized additive model (GAM; for larger datasets).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(comb\_df, }\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition, share)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_point}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{color =}\NormalTok{ system)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_smooth}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{color =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}black\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{facet\_wrap}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{vars}\NormalTok{(category), }\DataTypeTok{scales =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}free\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## `geom_smooth()` using method = 'loess' and formula 'y ~ x'
\end{verbatim}

\begin{center}\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-101-1} \end{center}

\hypertarget{discussion-question}{%
\subsubsection{Discussion question}\label{discussion-question}}

Incorporating a regression model into an exploratory analysis like this will almost always be a fraught decision. On the one hand, it aids pattern discovery, and isn't necessarily a radical step beyond plotting the data if we already think of the plot itself as a model. On the other hand, any regression model involves substantive assumptions, and it can be difficult to interrogate those assumptions when the model is used in this way.

What other considerations should we take into account when deciding whether to add a model to an exploratory plot?

\hypertarget{text-geoms}{%
\subsection{Text geoms}\label{text-geoms}}

The final plot highlights how easy it is to extend a traditional scatterplot using ggplot. First, the \texttt{text} geom prints strings of text (see also the \texttt{label} geom). This is especially useful when we want to identify individual observations but have too many values for colors or shapes. To accommodate the long labels, we expand the range of the x-values in the coordinate system and turn off clipping at the plot margins. We also scale the size of the points to campus size, which suggests a correlation between size (which in this case is tightly correlated with age and prestige) and Hispanic share. Aesthetics can be handled for the entire plot or separately for each layer, as needed to create effective cognitive affordances.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{comb\_df }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(system }\OperatorTok{==}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}UC\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, category }\OperatorTok{==}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}Hispanic\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{ggplot}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(in\_state\_tuition, share)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_point}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{size =}\NormalTok{ total\_enrollment), }\DataTypeTok{alpha =} \FloatTok{.5}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{geom\_text}\NormalTok{(}\KeywordTok{aes}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{label =}\NormalTok{ name)) }\OperatorTok{+}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{coord\_cartesian}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{clip =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}off\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\DataTypeTok{xlim =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\DecValTok{13000}\NormalTok{, }\DecValTok{16500}\NormalTok{))}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}
