\hypertarget{intermediate-visualization-in-r}{%
\section{Intermediate Visualization in R}\label{intermediate-visualization-in-r}}

This chapter is based on a \href{https://github.com/dsidavis/RGraphicsWorkshop}{workshop} that I ran at \href{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=grEmJSL4Lwc}{UC Davis} in February 2019. We won't be covering it in class. But it gives some more background on visualization in R and presents some more advanced uses of ggplot.

To work through the plots, I recommend commenting out everything and adding one layer at a time. This is the best way to understand how each layer modifies the plot.

Some of the plots include alternative layers, mostly geoms, to illustrate how different ways of representing the data provide different visual affordances.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{library}\NormalTok{(ggplot2)}

\NormalTok{data\_dir =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}data\textquotesingle{}}
\NormalTok{data\_files =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}papers\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}paper\_counts.Rds\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
               \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}stations\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}stations.Rds\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
               \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}stations.utm\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}stations.utm.Rds\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
               \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}temperature\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{ =}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}temp.Rds\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{data\_paths =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{file.path}\NormalTok{(data\_dir, data\_files)}
\KeywordTok{names}\NormalTok{(data\_paths) =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{names}\NormalTok{(data\_files)}
\NormalTok{data\_urls =}\StringTok{ }\KeywordTok{paste0}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}https://github.com/dsidavis/RGraphicsWorkshop/raw/master/data/\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, data\_files)}

\CommentTok{\#\# The repo includes a script \textasciigrave{}climate\_data.R\textasciigrave{} that creates the final 3 datasets}

\CommentTok{\#\# \textasciigrave{}if\textasciigrave{} isn\textquotesingle{}t vectorized, so we have to loop over this manually}
\NormalTok{download\_file =}\StringTok{ }\ControlFlowTok{function}\NormalTok{(path, url) \{}
    \ControlFlowTok{if}\NormalTok{ (}\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{file.exists}\NormalTok{(path)) \{}
        \KeywordTok{download.file}\NormalTok{(url, path)}
\NormalTok{    \}}
\NormalTok{\}}
\NormalTok{purrr}\OperatorTok{::}\KeywordTok{walk2}\NormalTok{(data\_paths, data\_urls, download\_file)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{base-graphics-vs.-ggplot2}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Base graphics vs.~\texttt{ggplot2}}{Base graphics vs.~ggplot2}}\label{base-graphics-vs.-ggplot2}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  R comes with a standard set of visualization tools, called \emph{base graphics}.\footnote{Strictly speaking, \texttt{base} is the name of a particular package; ``base graphics'' aren't included in this package, but instead are in the \texttt{graphics} package. So ``base graphics'' is strictly incorrect. But ``graphics graphics'' sounds silly, and the \texttt{graphics} package is loaded automatically when you launch R, so everyone just calls it ``base graphics.''}\\
\item
  Base graphics' approach to visualization is often called an ``artist's palette'' model

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A set of low-level tools

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{points()}, \texttt{lines()}, \texttt{polygon()}
    \item
      \texttt{abline()}, \texttt{curve()}
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Define a canvas first, then use these tools to paint elements on the canvas
  \item
    The \href{https://pbpython.com/visualization-tools-1.html}{common Python plotting packages} (\texttt{matplotlib}, \texttt{seaborn}, etc.) are object-oriented variations on this

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{matplotlib} was originally designed to work like plotting in MATLAB, in order to ease the transition for scientists coming from MATLAB
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Advantages of base graphics

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    One-liners

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{plot(some\_object)} often does something useful

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        Try it with dataframes, regression models, principal components results, hierarchical clusterings, igraph networks, \ldots{}
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      \texttt{hist()}, \texttt{pairs()}, \texttt{barplot()}, \texttt{boxplot()}, \texttt{pie()}(!)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Straightforward to extend to custom classes
  \item
    No\(^*\) dependencies; works on any\(^*\) R installation
  \item
    Very flexible for unusual visualizations or combinations of plots

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      triplots/barycentric coordinates
    \item
      scatterplots with marginal densities
    \item
      heatmaps with marginal dendrograms
    \item
      map with inset plots of local data
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Disadvantages of base graphics

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Parameters aren't perspicuous

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      fill color is \texttt{bg} (background?)
    \item
      point shape is \texttt{pch} (uhhh point-character?)
    \item
      point and text size is \texttt{cex} (???)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    ``Ugly defaults''
  \item
    Plots as \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Side_effect_(computer_science)}{side effects}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Advantages of \texttt{ggplot2}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Conceptual model
  \item
    Facets
  \item
    Themes and palettes
  \item
    Plots are \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First-class_citizen}{first-class objects}
  \item
    ``Pretty defaults'' (though I disagree)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Disadvantages of \texttt{ggplot2}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Only\(^*\) for data frames in \href{https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/tidyr/vignettes/tidy-data.html}{\emph{long format}}
  \item
    Programming with \texttt{ggplot2} requires understanding \href{https://www.tidyverse.org/blog/2018/07/ggplot2-tidy-evaluation/}{tidy evaluation}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{plot-1-dumbbell-plot}{%

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist

\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{discussion}{%
\subsubsection{Discussion}\label{discussion}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  What design decisions are being used to highlight the message of this plot?\\
\item
  What alternative designs could be used to communicate the same message?

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Are any of these alternative designs better than the one I used?\\
  \item
    Try implementing your idea in ggplot
  \end{itemize}
\item
  How does this plot omit, distort, or mislead?
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{plot-4-visualizing-spatial-data-in-r}{%
\subsection{Plot 4: Visualizing spatial data in R}\label{plot-4-visualizing-spatial-data-in-r}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  I don't recommend visualizing spatial data until you've learned a bit about coordinate systems and projections
\item
  Definitely don't try to do math on spatial coordinates unless you know what you're doing!
\item
  The \texttt{sf} package for working with spatial data has a ton of dependencies, so I'm going to disable all of the code blocks below.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    They're here for you to explore on your own if interested.\\
  \item
    \textbf{This code has not been checked for errors.}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  As of version 3, \texttt{ggplot2} can plot vector spatial data from \texttt{sf} objects using \texttt{geom\_sf()}
\item
  This section looks at two different ways of adding basemaps under \texttt{geom\_sf()} plots
\end{itemize}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Services other than Google Maps are deprecated
\item
  Requires Google account, API key
\item
  Doesn't seem to play nicely with coordinates other than lat/lon
\item
  See \texttt{?register\_google} for links and instructions
\item
  \textbf{\emph{DO NOT SHARE YOUR API KEY}}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{part-reproducibility}{%
\part{Reproducibility}\label{part-reproducibility}}

\hypertarget{reproducibility-replicability-and-robustness}{%
\section{Reproducibility, Replicability, and Robustness}\label{reproducibility-replicability-and-robustness}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Reading: Yong (\protect\hyperlink{ref-YongReplicationStudiesBad2012}{2012}), Aschwanden (\protect\hyperlink{ref-AschwandenThereNoSuch2017}{2017}), Hicks (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{2020}\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{b})
\item
  Highly recommended: Feest (\protect\hyperlink{ref-FeestWhyReplicationOverrated2019}{2019}), Leonelli (\protect\hyperlink{ref-LeonelliRethinkingReproducibilityCriterion2018}{2018})
\item
  Some data management disasters

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    (\emph{The Economist} \protect\hyperlink{ref-ArrayErrors2011}{2011}), (``Video: Keith Baggerly, "When Is Reproducibility an Ethical Issue? Genomics, Personalized Medicine, and Human Error"'' \protect\hyperlink{ref-VideoKeithBaggerly}{n.d.})
  \item
    Herndon, Ash, and Pollin (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HerndonDoesHighPublic2014}{2014}), but you can just read Bailey and Borwein (Jon) (\protect\hyperlink{ref-BaileyReinhartRogoffErrorHow}{n.d.}), Cassidy (\protect\hyperlink{ref-CassidyReinhartRogoffControversy}{n.d.}), and/or watch (\emph{Reinhart \& Rogoff - Growth in a Time of Debt - EXERCISE!} \protect\hyperlink{ref-ReinhartRogoffGrowth2019}{2019})
  \item
    Laskowski (\protect\hyperlink{ref-LaskowskiWhatWhenYou}{n.d.}), Viglione (\protect\hyperlink{ref-ViglioneAvalancheSpiderpaperRetractions2020}{2020}), Pennisi (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PennisiProminentSpiderBiologist2020}{2020})
  \item
    (``How Excel May Have Caused Loss of 16,000 Covid Tests in England'' \protect\hyperlink{ref-HowExcelMay2020}{n.d.})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{the-replication-crisis}{%
\subsection{The Replication Crisis}\label{the-replication-crisis}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  An epistemic-methodological crisis unfolding across multiple scientific fields, especially biomedicine and social psychology
\item
  Usually defined in a phrase: ``studies replicate much less often than you would expect''
\item
  Received the most attention in the wake of a high-profile effort to replicate a collection of high-profile psychology studies (Open Science Collaboration \protect\hyperlink{ref-OpenScienceCollaborationEstimatingReproducibilityPsychological2015}{2015})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    But it's been simmering for a long time (Ioannidis \protect\hyperlink{ref-IoannidisWhyMostPublished2005}{2005}; Meehl \protect\hyperlink{ref-MeehlTheoryTestingPsychologyPhysics1967}{1967})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  In psychology

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Has stimulated a move towards preregistered studies, open science, and lots of papers reflecting on psychological methods
  \item
    Open science movement: Nosek et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-NosekPromotingOpenResearch2015}{2015}); Munafò et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-MunafoManifestoReproducibleScience2017}{2017})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/reproducibility/badges} 

}

\caption{As part of the open science movement, some journals have added badges to journal articles indicating open data, open code, etc.}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-111}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Beyond academia

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    In my experience most members of the general public aren't aware of it
  \item
    Even scientists in non-experimental fields (e.g., cultural anthropologists) have forgotten
  \item
    But it has been used in policy settings to manufacture doubt about environmental epidemiology (Aschwanden \protect\hyperlink{ref-AschwandenThereNoSuch2017}{2017}; Hicks \protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{2020}\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{b})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{terminology}{%
\subsection{Terminology}\label{terminology}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  A 2019 National Academy of Sciences (NAS) report noted that people often use the terms ``reproduce'' and ``replicate'' interchangeably, and consequently conflate two very different expectations for quantitative experimental research (National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine \protect\hyperlink{ref-NationalAcademiesofSciencesEngineeringandMedicineReproducibilityReplicabilityScience2019}{2019})
\item
  Hicks (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{2020}\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{b}) reviews the NAS definitions and also compares them to ``robustness''

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Replicability}: Run the experimental again, getting new data on a more-or-less similar sample. Do the same analysis. Do we get qualitatively similar results?\\
  \item
    \textbf{Reproducibility}: Run the same analysis script on the same data on a different computer. Do we get numerically identical results?
  \item
    \textbf{Robustness}: Use a \emph{different} analytical approach to attempt to answer the same research question on the \emph{same} data. Do we get qualitatively similar results? (Steegen et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-SteegenIncreasingTransparencyMultiverse2016}{2016}; Silberzahn et al. \protect\hyperlink{ref-SilberzahnManyAnalystsOne2018}{2018})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lccl@{}}
\caption{The distinction between replicability, reproducibility, and robustness can be made by considering whether we use the same data, the same analysis, and whether we expect to get numerically identical or merely similar results.}\tabularnewline
\toprule
& same data? & same analysis? & expect to get\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
& same data? & same analysis? & expect to get\tabularnewline
\midrule
\endhead
replicability & & Y & similar results\tabularnewline
reproducibility & Y & Y & numerically identical results\tabularnewline
robustness & Y & & similar results\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  ``Qualitatively similar'' is necessarily vague and requires interpretation

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Researchers can and do disagree about whether any particular set of results is sufficiently ``similar'' or ``different'' (Feest \protect\hyperlink{ref-FeestExperimentersRegressReconsidered2016}{2016})
  \item
    Sometimes a quantitative standard is used: ``replication attempt is also statistically significant and effect is in the same direction''
  \item
    But the unavoidable chance of type I and II errors means there's still uncertainty
  \item
    And remember that the threshold of statistical significance is basically arbitrary
  \end{itemize}
\item
  ``Numerically identical'' is not vague

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    (At least, up to a tolerance threshold, and with the proviso that the computers are working correctly)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Problem-solution mismatch

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    The replication crisis was spurred by high-profile \emph{replication} failures
  \item
    But open science is about promoting \emph{reproducibility} (Hicks \protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{2020}\protect\hyperlink{ref-HicksCommentSubmittedDaniel2020}{b})
  \item
    Connection between reproducibility and replication is generally quite thin
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{epistemic-value-of-reproducibility}{%
\subsection{Epistemic Value of Reproducibility}\label{epistemic-value-of-reproducibility}}

Reproducibility is a \emph{necessary} condition for computational research, but has \emph{low evidentiary value}.

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Necessary condition: If we can't run your code on your data and get the same output, something has gone seriously wrong.
\item
  Low evidentiary value: Reproducibility tells us \emph{only} that your code reliably produces the output you reported.
\end{itemize}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}l@{}}
\caption{Things reproducibility doesn't tell us}\tabularnewline
\toprule
\endhead
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your research question is reasonable\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your data was collected the way you think it was\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your data can possibly answer your research question\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your data was fabricated\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your data was mismanaged\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your analyses are appropriate\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your analysis includes relevant covariates\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether your code runs the analysis that you think it does\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
Whether you p-hacked your analysis (Simmons, Nelson, and Simonsohn \protect\hyperlink{ref-SimmonsFalsePositivePsychology2011}{2011})\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
How to interpret your analysis output\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.97\columnwidth}\raggedright
How to characterize your intervention and endpoint (Feest \protect\hyperlink{ref-FeestWhyReplicationOverrated2019}{2019})\strut
\end{minipage}\tabularnewline
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\hypertarget{open-science-reproducible}{%
\subsection{Open Science ≠ Reproducible}\label{open-science-reproducible}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Obels et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-ObelsAnalysisOpenData2020}{2020})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Reproducibility check of 62 pre-registered studies in a Center for Open Science database
  \item
    ``Of the 62 articles that met our inclusion criteria, 41 had data available, and 37 had analysis scripts available''
  \item
    ``We could run the scripts for 31 analyses, and we reproduced the main results for 21 articles.''
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\textbackslash begin\{figure\}

\{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{book_files/figure-latex/unnamed-chunk-112-1}

\}

\textbackslash caption\{Infographic for Obels et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-ObelsAnalysisOpenData2020}{2020}). While 58\% of preregistered studies provided both the data and analysis script, only 50\% had runnable scripts and only 34\% could actually be reproduced.\}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-112}
\textbackslash end\{figure\}

\hypertarget{reproducibility-and-software-engineering}{%
\section{Reproducibility and Software Engineering}\label{reproducibility-and-software-engineering}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Reading: Postolovski (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PostolovskiYourCodeReview2020}{2020}); Wilson et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WilsonGoodEnoughPractices2017}{2017}); Buckens (\protect\hyperlink{ref-BuckensSelfdocumentingMythHow2019}{2019})
\item
  Recommended video: McElreath (\protect\hyperlink{ref-McElreathScienceAmateurSoftware2020}{2020})
\item
  The reproducibility side of the replication crisis suggests that academic science has a reproducibility problem
\item
  But software engineering doesn't
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{what-can-we-learn-from-software-engineering}{%
\subsection{What can we learn from software engineering?}\label{what-can-we-learn-from-software-engineering}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Things we've already talked about

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    develop in a clean workspace

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      restart and rerun often
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    version control
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Things we'll talk about in this section of the course

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    using a style guide and linter
  \item
    the life-changing magic of tidying up (your files)
  \item
    code review
  \item
    documenting code and data
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Things we'll talk about later, time permitting

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{make} and continuous integration for automating reproducibility
  \item
    unit testing for validating code
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{code-style}{%
\subsection{Code style}\label{code-style}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  iNéwritteNélanguagEáconventionSéfoRîpunctuatioNøcapitalizatioNîaiDécomprehensioNébYéindicatinGéstructurE

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    this is what it's like to read poorly-styled code
  \item
    conventions only work if they're \emph{shared} conventions
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Style guides provide shared conventions for readable code

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    In-line spacing makes it easier to pick out distinguish functions, operators, and variables in a line
  \item
    Returns distinguish arguments in a function call
  \item
    Indentation corresponds to structure of complex expressions
  \item
    Common conventions for naming, assignment reduce cognitive load
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Tidyverse style guide: \url{https://style.tidyverse.org/}
\item
  \texttt{lintr}: \url{https://cran.r-project.org/web/packages/lintr/index.html}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{organizing-your-projects}{%
\section{Organizing your projects}\label{organizing-your-projects}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  Reading: Noble (\protect\hyperlink{ref-NobleQuickGuideOrganizing2009}{2009})
\item
  See also: \url{https://support.rstudio.com/hc/en-us/articles/200526207-Using-Projects}
\item
  Good project organization will

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Keep your project self-contained, making it easy to find files as needed
  \item
    Play nicely with version control
  \item
    Self-document key relationships between project files
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

While we're at it, put your work in folders, not scattered across your desktop.

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/reproducibility/folder_structure} 

}

\caption{Folder organization is your friend. Most of my work lives in Google Drive for automatic backups. I have top-level folders for teaching, coding, and writing projects. Then each project or collection of projects lives in its own folder. I take advantage of OS features such as shortcuts, highlighting, and special characters in file names to make it easy to find frequently-used things. An 'Archives' folder is good for tucking old projects out of the way.}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-113}
\end{figure}

\hypertarget{two-templates}{%
\subsection{Two templates}\label{two-templates}}

\hypertarget{noblequickguideorganizing2009}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Noble (\protect\hyperlink{ref-NobleQuickGuideOrganizing2009}{2009})}{Noble (2009)}}\label{noblequickguideorganizing2009}}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/reproducibility/noble} 

}

\caption{Noble's [-@NobleQuickGuideOrganizing2009] sample folder structure is designed for experimental biologists.}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-114}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Includes code for

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    running experiments
  \item
    cleaning and analyzing data
  \item
    typesetting a LateX file into a PDF
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Other features

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    notebook file, updated regularly
  \item
    chronological folders for experimental runs
  \item
    folders for compiled code and their corresponding source files
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{djhs-project-template}{%
\subsubsection{DJH's Project Template}\label{djhs-project-template}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \url{https://github.com/dhicks/project_template}
\item
  Configured as a GitHub ``template,'' making it easy to create new repositories for new projects
\item
  Designated folders for data, plots/outputs, and utility functions
\item
  File naming convention

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Automatically orders analysis scripts
  \item
    Coordinates plots, outputs, and intermediate data with the script that creates them
  \end{itemize}
\item
  R package infrastructure

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Enables one-liner to load the utility functions in \texttt{R}: \texttt{devtools::load\_all(\textquotesingle{}..\textquotesingle{})}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{a-note-on-paths}{%
\subsection{A Note on Paths}\label{a-note-on-paths}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Windows and Unix-based systems write paths differently
\item
  Use \texttt{file.path()} or the \texttt{here} package to construct paths
\item
  \texttt{..} in a path means ``go up to the parent folder''

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    so \texttt{../data/00\_raw\_data.csv} goes up one level (eg, from the \texttt{scripts} folder), then down to the \texttt{data} folder, then the file \texttt{00\_raw\_data.csv}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{code-review}{%
\section{Code review}\label{code-review}}

\hypertarget{a-code-review-checklist}{%
\subsection{A code review checklist}\label{a-code-review-checklist}}

Postolovski (\protect\hyperlink{ref-PostolovskiYourCodeReview2020}{2020})

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Readability / understandability}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://style.tidyverse.org/files.html}{File naming}
  \item
    Follows a \href{https://style.tidyverse.org/}{style guide}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Or at least uses consistent style
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Whitespace, whitespace, whitespace
  \item
    Meaningful names / \href{https://dev.to/woubuc/self-documenting-is-a-myth-and-how-to-make-your-code-self-documenting-3h2n}{self-documenting code}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Maintainability}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Don\%27t_repeat_yourself}{DRY}
  \item
    Avoid hard-coded configuration
  \item
    Check strong assumptions about structure and content of inputs
  \item
    Avoid deprecated or removed features
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Security

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    How are passwords for websites, APIs, and sensitive data handled?
  \item
    Data validation
  \item
    Code validation (tests and assertions)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Speed and Performance

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Performance for users

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{tictoc} for simple wall time checks
    \item
      Profiling for more thorough optimization
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Resource use

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Does this really need to be run on the MERCED cluster?
    \item
      How much will this cost the lab/team if it's accidentally run 1,000 times?
    \item
      Cache raw returns from web scraping and API queries
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Documentation}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Scripts: Section markers, signposting of what's happening and why
  \item
    Functions: Assumptions about inputs, explanation of output

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Consider using \texttt{roxygen2} + R's package infrastructure
    \item
      \url{https://r-pkgs.org/man.html\#man-functions}
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Data: Provenance, date and time retrieved, codebook
  \item
    Project: README, NEWS
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Reinventing the Wheel

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Is there a (well-maintained, checked) package for this?
  \item
    \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Don\%27t_repeat_yourself}{DRY}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Reliability}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    What happens if packages aren't installed?

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Reminder: \texttt{require()} doesn't stop if the package is missing
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Data can't be found?
  \item
    Data has missing values or errors?
  \item
    Data has been altered or corrupted?
  \item
    A function returns a missing or zero-length value?
  \item
    One script in the middle of the pipeline is altered?
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Scalability
\item
  Reusability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Can this bit of code be dropped into the lab's/team's next project?
  \end{itemize}
\item
  In-house/lab/team style
\item
  Test coverage and quality

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Unpopular opinion: For ``bench data science,'' assertions often make more sense than unit tests
  \item
    \texttt{assertthat} for assertions; \texttt{testthat} for unit tests
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Fit for purpose}
\item
  Notice what's missing

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Edge cases, unexpected inputs, error handling
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Zoom out
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{an-example-code-review}{%
\subsection{An Example Code Review}\label{an-example-code-review}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  We'll review the code for Colombo et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-ColomboIntellectuallyHumblePrejudiced2020}{2020})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Data and code available at \url{https://osf.io/k5qmw/}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  I find a lot of problems

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    But don't read this as criticizing the authors
  \item
    These types of problems are widespread in computational science
  \item
    Because researchers aren't trained to look for, prevent, and correct these problems
  \item
    \textbf{Take home message: Do code review as part of your research and it won't be this painful}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{a-shortened-checklist}{%
\subsubsection{A shortened checklist}\label{a-shortened-checklist}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{-1}
\tightlist
\item
  What happens if you just run the script?
\item
  Readability
\item
  Maintainability
\item
  Documentation
\item
  Reliability
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{project-organization}{%
\subsubsection{Project organization}\label{project-organization}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  No documentation

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    README, attribution
  \item
    Data provenance, codebook
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Folder and file names

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Folder names suggest an order
  \item
    Which files are inputs and which are outputs?
  \item
    Date stamps in file names - was version control used?
  \item
    Inconsistent styles in file names
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Are the inputs for the \texttt{meta} analysis the outputs from the studies?

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    What happens if something changes in the study analyses?
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{study1}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{study1}}{study1}}\label{study1}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{-1}
\tightlist
\item
  Just run the script

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Error on line 16
  \item
    Trying to take a subset of columns from the data being read in
  \item
    Using column indices
  \item
    But the indices don't match the number of columns in the data
  \item
    We can't recover from this without documentation for the data

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      What columns should be in the raw data, and what columns will be used for the analysis?
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Readability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Long lines, inconsistent whitespace
  \item
    Repetitive chunks of analysis aren't documented

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Could be put into function(s) with meaningful names
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Maintainability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Lots of repetition: lines 25-41; analysis on 90-95; numerous places throughout
  \item
    Doesn't seem like this analysis is being written out anywhere?

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      In particular, how is the output here supposed to feed into the meta-analysis?
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Line 132: why are we deleting things?
  \item
    \texttt{cor.mtest} is defined multiple times
  \item
    Line 265: surprise \texttt{library()} call
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Documentation

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Line 46: Looks like we're starting one section of the analysis

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      But it's easy to miss this section break, similar breaks eg line 134
    \item
      Contrast very visible break on 199
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    What are these tests, plots, and why are we doing them?
  \item
    Contrast with the factor analysis starting on line 79
  \item
    Lines 116-17: Did they solve the ``this is bad''?
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Reliability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A quick check with \texttt{skimr} and/or \texttt{visdat} for study 1 indicates lots of missing values for a few variables

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      maybe because respondents rated different numbers of groups (why?)
    \item
      also some sporadic missingness
    \item
      doesn't seem like this was handled explicitly
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    No checks to validate data
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{study2}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{study2}}{study2}}\label{study2}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{-1}
\tightlist
\item
  Just run the script

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Syntax error on line 282

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Uncommenting 281 gives a very cryptic error
    \item
      Commenting out these lines -\textgreater{} everything seems to run
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    But should we be worried about all of these convergence warnings?
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Pretty much all the same concerns as with \texttt{study1}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Seems to use exactly the same analysis functions

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      These should be defined once, in a single R file called by the analysis scripts
    \item
      Could even be a local package loaded using \texttt{usethis::load\_all()} (\url{https://devtools.r-lib.org/reference/load_all.html})
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{study3}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{study3}}{study3}}\label{study3}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{-1}
\tightlist
\item
  Just run the script

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Line 47: Missing dependency
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Readability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Long lines, bad pipe style
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Maintainability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Again, issues with repetition, not writing things out
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Documentation

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Basically none
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Reliability

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{TRUE} and \texttt{FALSE} are reserved words, meaning they can't be used as variable names

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{T} and \texttt{F} are variables defined by default to match these
    \item
      Their value can be changed
    \item
      So using \texttt{TRUE} and \texttt{FALSE} is preferred
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Several repetitions of \texttt{export(tidy(m6k,\ conf.int\ \ =\ T),\ "export.xlsx")}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Writing over \texttt{m6k} each time with a new regression model
    \item
      Is \texttt{export()} appending the results to \texttt{export.xlsx}, or overwriting the file?
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{study4-and-meta}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\texttt{study4} and \texttt{meta}}{study4 and meta}}\label{study4-and-meta}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Same issues as the other script files
\item
  Confirm that the input for the meta-analysis isn't directly generated from the study scripts
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{code-review-activity}{%
\section{Code Review Activity}\label{code-review-activity}}

For this activity, you'll be working in pairs on a two-part assignment. In the first part, you'll do a brief exploratory data analysis of the dataset used in
Silberzahn and Uhlmann (\protect\hyperlink{ref-SilberzahnCrowdsourcedResearchMany2015}{2015}) and Silberzahn et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-SilberzahnManyAnalystsOne2018}{2018}). In the second part, you and your partner will do a code review for another pair's EDA.

\hypertarget{a-shorter-checklist}{%
\subsection{A shorter checklist}\label{a-shorter-checklist}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{-1}
\tightlist
\item
  What happens if you just run the script?
\item
  Readability
\item
  Maintainability
\item
  Documentation
\item
  Reliability
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{part-1}{%
\subsection{Part 1}\label{part-1}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Set up the project

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    One member of your group should fork the (nearly empty) repository for this activity: \url{https://github.com/data-science-methods/code-review}
  \item
    Create appropriate folders, a README with your names and contact information and a short explanation, etc.
  \item
    Download the data files and situate them in your repository.

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      The data file itself: \url{https://osf.io/fv8c3/}
    \item
      The documentation, which should be named \texttt{README.rtf}: \url{https://osf.io/9yh4x/}
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    In the repository on GitHub, go to the Settings tab and look for Manage Access on the left-hand side. Use the ``Invite a collaborator'' button to give everyone in your group permission to push up to the repo.

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Otherwise you'll have to deal with a lot of pull requests.
    \item
      See the screenshot for the elements you're looking for.
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/reproducibility/collaborators} 

}

\caption{To give someone permission to push to a repository, go to Settings, then Manage Access, then Invite a collaborator.}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-115}
\end{figure}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\setcounter{enumi}{1}
\tightlist
\item
  Start working on an EDA of the data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Use the checklists and tools from our EDA unit (ch.~9).
  \item
    You can use the primary question from Silberzahn et al.: \emph{Are dark-skinned football (soccer) players more likely to be red-carded?}
  \item
    \textbf{Pair programming}: Either work in person if you can, or using screenshare over Zoom. Switch off which person is typing every 15 minutes or so. Using GitHub to sync your work between your computers.
  \item
    You'll have \emph{about 90 minutes} to work on this.
  \item
    There are three goals here, in order:

    \begin{enumerate}
    \def\labelenumii{\arabic{enumii}.}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Practice writing good, readable code
    \item
      Generate example code for Part 2
    \item
      Practice using the checklists and tools for EDA
    \end{enumerate}
  \item
    Don't worry if things are ``incomplete.''
  \end{itemize}
\item
  File a PR against the original repository.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{part-2}{%
\subsection{Part 2}\label{part-2}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  I'll send you a link to another group's repository.
\item
  Working with your partner, review the code in the PR.

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Clone the other group's repository to your machine to run it.
  \item
    Go to the ``Files changed'' tab and browse through the code, adding comments in a code review.
  \item
    \emph{Use the short checklist at the top of this page}: Relate each comment to one element of the checklist.
  \item
    \textbf{Remember that these are your classmates and all of this is public.}
  \item
    Frame your points as praise, suggestions, and constructive criticism.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  When you're ready to submit your review, include a summary comment noting the most important changes that you would recommend.
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{part-3}{%
\subsection{Part 3}\label{part-3}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  After the other group submits their summary, review their comments and suggestions, and make changes as appropriate.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{managing-and-publishing-data}{%
\section{Managing and Publishing Data}\label{managing-and-publishing-data}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  reading:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Wilkinson et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WilkinsonFAIRGuidingPrinciples2016}{2016})
  \item
    (``Open Data Offer Risks and Rewards for Conservation'' \protect\hyperlink{ref-OpenDataOffer2018}{2018})
  \item
    Nicol et al. (\protect\hyperlink{ref-NicolConsentInsufficientData2019}{2019})
  \item
    McCook (\protect\hyperlink{ref-McCookPubliclyAvailableData2016}{2016})
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{fair-principles-for-published-data}{%
\subsection{FAIR principles for published data}\label{fair-principles-for-published-data}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \emph{Findable}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    F1. (meta)data are assigned a globally unique and persistent identifier
  \item
    F2. data are described with rich metadata (defined by R1 below)
  \item
    F3. metadata clearly and explicitly include the identifier of the data it describes
  \item
    F4. (meta)data are registered or indexed in a searchable resource
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Accessible}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    A1. (meta)data are retrievable by their identifier using a standardized communications protocol

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      A1.1 the protocol is open, free, and universally implementable
    \item
      A1.2 the protocol allows for an authentication and authorization procedure, where necessary
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    A2. metadata are accessible, even when the data are no longer available
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Interoperable}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    I1. (meta)data use a formal, accessible, shared, and broadly applicable language for knowledge representation.
  \item
    I2. (meta)data use vocabularies that follow FAIR principles
  \item
    I3. (meta)data include qualified references to other (meta)data
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{Reusable}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    R1. meta(data) are richly described with a plurality of accurate and relevant attributes

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      R1.1. (meta)data are released with a clear and accessible data usage license
    \item
      R1.2. (meta)data are associated with detailed provenance
    \item
      R1.3. (meta)data meet domain-relevant community standards
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  DOIs for data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://help.osf.io/hc/en-us/articles/360019931013-Create-DOIs}{Instructions for OSF}
  \item
    \href{https://help.zenodo.org/\#versioning}{Notes for Zenodo}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \href{https://guides.github.com/activities/citable-code/}{Zenodo also plays nicely with GitHub for minting DOIs for code}
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    \href{https://dataverse.org/best-practices/data-citation}{Citation models at Harvard Dataverse}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{documentation}{%
\subsection{Documentation}\label{documentation}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Many social science fields have a tradition of writing codebooks for their data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://github.com/stanford-policylab/opp/blob/master/data_readme.md}{Stanford Open Policing codebook}
  \item
    \href{https://kjhealy.github.io/covdata/articles/codebook.html}{``Codebook-like summary'' of the \texttt{covdata} package, automatically generated using \texttt{skimr}}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Major codebook elements (\url{https://afit-r.github.io/codebook})

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Original source of the data
  \item
    Sampling information

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Where and how the data were generated
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Variable-level metadata and summaries
  \item
    Structure of the data
  \end{enumerate}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{data-management-plans}{%
\subsection{Data management plans}\label{data-management-plans}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Much like a research plan, data management plans provide an overview of the steps you'll take to gather, publish, and maintain your data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Since 2011, NSF has required a 2-page data management plan for most types of proposals
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Examples and resources

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://library.ucmerced.edu/research/researchers/research-data-management/data-management-plans}{UCM Library}
  \item
    \href{https://it.ucmerced.edu/content/research-data-management-consultation-service}{UCM IT support}
  \item
    \href{https://libraries.ucsd.edu/research-and-collections/data-curation/dmp-samples.html}{NSF examples}
  \item
    \href{https://www.nsf.gov/bfa/dias/policy/dmp.jsp}{NSF policy summary}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{hazards-of-open-data}{%
\subsection{Hazards of open data}\label{hazards-of-open-data}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Legal

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Privacy

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Personally identifiable information (PII): ``information which can be used to \textbf{distinguish or trace an individual's identity}, such as their name, social security number, biometric records, etc. \emph{alone, or when combined with other \ldots{} information}'' \href{https://www.osec.doc.gov/opog/privacy/PII_BII.html}{Source}
    \item
      Sensitive information: ``if the loss, compromission {[}sic{]}, or disclosure without authorization of this data could \textbf{result in harm, embarrassment, inconvenience, or unfairness} to an individual'' \href{https://techgdpr.com/blog/difference-between-pii-and-personal-data/}{Source}
    \item
      IRB will require you to

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        indicate what PII and sensitive information you're collecting
      \item
        explain why you need to collect it
      \item
        outline a plan to keep it secure
      \item
        notify or mitigate issues with participants if security is compromised
      \end{itemize}
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Copyright

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      Data that you've bought from a broker
    \item
      Journal article text, \emph{but also} article metadata if you used a private service to retrieve it
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Community-level hazards

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Location of endangered species (``Open Data Offer Risks and Rewards for Conservation'' \protect\hyperlink{ref-OpenDataOffer2018}{2018})
  \item
    Indigenous community sacred sites
  \item
    Antiquities
  \item
    Identifying the Golden State Killer through GEDmatch (Zhang \protect\hyperlink{ref-ZhangHowTinyWebsite2018}{2018})
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Public-but-inaccessible data

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    OKCupid data scrape (McCook \protect\hyperlink{ref-McCookPubliclyAvailableData2016}{2016})

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \href{https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Emil_Kirkegaard}{Don't be like Emil O. Kierkegaard}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{some-data-cleaning-tools}{%
\section{Some Data-Cleaning Tools}\label{some-data-cleaning-tools}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  reading: Wickham (\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamTidyData2014}{2014}\protect\hyperlink{ref-WickhamTidyData2014}{a})
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{tidy-data}{%
\subsection{Tidy Data}\label{tidy-data}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  conventions for organizing data for analysis

  \begin{enumerate}
  \def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Each variable forms a column.
  \item
    Each observation forms a row.
  \item
    Each type of observational unit forms a table.
  \end{enumerate}
\item
  Example: student demographics and major, for students who took a philosophy course at UC Davis, 2005-2015
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/data/tidy-1} \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{images/data/tidy-2} 

}

\caption{Two tables in a tidy dataset. The first table records students' majors during each term they were enrolled; the second records demographics.  The tables have different units of observation, but can be linked using the ID (internal to this dataset) for an analysis at the level of individual students. Other tables in this dataset include final grades for philosophy courses taken by the students, and demographics of the instructors of those courses.}\label{fig:unnamed-chunk-117}
\end{figure}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  slightly rephrased

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    1 table = 1 unit of observation

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      student-term (major)
    \item
      student (demographics)
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    1 row = 1 unique observed unit

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      1 row = 1 student-term combination
    \item
      1 row = 1 student
    \end{itemize}
  \item
    Every column is either an \emph{identifier} or a \emph{measured variable}

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      rule of thumb: 1 row = 1 unique combination of identifiers
    \item
      major table

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        identifiers: student ID, \texttt{term}
      \item
        measured variables: admit term, relative term, major
      \item
        NB the \texttt{term} identifier is constructed from two measured variables
      \end{itemize}
    \item
      demographics table

      \begin{itemize}
      \tightlist
      \item
        identifier: student ID
      \item
        measured variables: gender, race and ethnicity variables, first gen status, low income status, admission type, whether they ever majored in philosophy, major the semester they took their first philosophy course
      \item
        NB here ``measured variable'' includes both variables in the raw data as well as variables I constructed for analysis purposes (\texttt{poc}, \texttt{demographic})
      \end{itemize}
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{tools-for-tidying-data}{%

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{\#\textquotesingle{} Helper function to parse weeks to integers in the Billboard dataset}
\CommentTok{\#\textquotesingle{} @param wk\_chr Vector of week characters, with the pattern \textasciigrave{}c(\textquotesingle{}wk1\textquotesingle{}, \textquotesingle{}wk2\textquotesingle{})\textasciigrave{}}
\CommentTok{\#\textquotesingle{} @return Vector of integers, eg, \textasciigrave{}c(1L, 2L)\textasciigrave{}}
\NormalTok{parse\_week =}\StringTok{ }\ControlFlowTok{function}\NormalTok{(wk\_chr) \{}
\NormalTok{    wk\_int =}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{wk\_chr }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{        }\KeywordTok{str\_remove}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}wk\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{        }\KeywordTok{as.integer}\NormalTok{()}
    \KeywordTok{return}\NormalTok{(wk\_int)}
\NormalTok{\}}

\NormalTok{billboard\_clean =}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{billboard\_df }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Drop the row number column}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{select}\NormalTok{(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{X1) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Pivot week down and remove NA rows}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{pivot\_longer}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{cols =} \KeywordTok{starts\_with}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}wk\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
                 \DataTypeTok{names\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}week\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
                 \DataTypeTok{values\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}rank\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(}\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{is.na}\NormalTok{(rank)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Parse week and calculate date}
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# NB Base R can handle date arithmetic}
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# For month arithmetic see ?lubridate::\textasciigrave{}\%m+\%\textasciigrave{}}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{week =} \KeywordTok{parse\_week}\NormalTok{(week), }
           \DataTypeTok{date =}\NormalTok{ date.entered }\OperatorTok{+}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{(week}\DecValTok{{-}1}\NormalTok{)}\OperatorTok{*}\DecValTok{7}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Clean columns}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{select}\NormalTok{(year, artist, time, track, date, week, rank)}
\KeywordTok{head}\NormalTok{(billboard\_clean)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

NB. Base R includes methods for doing date arithmetic, eg,

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\KeywordTok{as.Date}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}2020{-}04{-}30\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{+}\StringTok{ }\DecValTok{1}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{verbatim}
## [1] "2020-05-01"
\end{verbatim}

If you need month arithmetic, check out the \texttt{lubridate} package, and specifically \texttt{\%m+\%} and related operators.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{tb\_clean =}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{tb\_df }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# One row per observation = country x gender x age group}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{pivot\_longer}\NormalTok{(m014}\OperatorTok{:}\NormalTok{fu, }
                 \DataTypeTok{names\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}column\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
                 \DataTypeTok{values\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}cases\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Separate gender and age}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{separate}\NormalTok{(column, }\DataTypeTok{into =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}gender\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
             \DataTypeTok{sep =} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Wrangle age: }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# {-} Split into upper and lower bounds}
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# {-} Recombine with {-}}
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# {-} Fix missing age and 65+}
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# {-} Set order}
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# {-} Clean up the intermediate columns for upper and lower bounds}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{separate}\NormalTok{(age, }\DataTypeTok{into =} \KeywordTok{c}\NormalTok{(}\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\_lower\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}age\_upper\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
             \DataTypeTok{sep =} \DecValTok{{-}2}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{age =} \KeywordTok{str\_c}\NormalTok{(age\_lower, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}{-}\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, age\_upper), }
           \DataTypeTok{age =} \KeywordTok{case\_when}\NormalTok{(age }\OperatorTok{==}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}{-}u\textquotesingle{}}  \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\StringTok{ }\OtherTok{NA\_character\_}\NormalTok{, }
\NormalTok{                           age }\OperatorTok{==}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}{-}65\textquotesingle{}} \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\StringTok{ \textquotesingle{}65+\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{, }
                           \OtherTok{TRUE}         \OperatorTok{\textasciitilde{}}\StringTok{ }\NormalTok{age), }
           \DataTypeTok{age =} \KeywordTok{fct\_inorder}\NormalTok{(age)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{select}\NormalTok{(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{age\_lower, }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{age\_upper) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Put in order, with ID columns first and then the variable}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{select}\NormalTok{(country, year, gender, age, cases)}
\KeywordTok{head}\NormalTok{(tb\_clean)}
\end{Highlighting}

                 \DataTypeTok{values\_to =} \StringTok{\textquotesingle{}obs\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Widen \textasciigrave{}element\textasciigrave{}}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{pivot\_wider}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{names\_from =}\NormalTok{ element, }
                \DataTypeTok{values\_from =}\NormalTok{ obs) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Fix the class of \textasciigrave{}day\textasciigrave{}}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{mutate}\NormalTok{(}\DataTypeTok{day =} \KeywordTok{str\_remove}\NormalTok{(day, }\StringTok{\textquotesingle{}d\textquotesingle{}}\NormalTok{), }
           \DataTypeTok{day =} \KeywordTok{as.integer}\NormalTok{(day)) }\OperatorTok{\%\textgreater{}\%}\StringTok{ }
\StringTok{    }\CommentTok{\#\# Check against the paper by filtering to non{-}missing rows}
\StringTok{    }\KeywordTok{filter}\NormalTok{(}\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{is.na}\NormalTok{(tmax) }\OperatorTok{|}\StringTok{ }\OperatorTok{!}\KeywordTok{is.na}\NormalTok{(tmin))}
\KeywordTok{head}\NormalTok{(weather\_clean)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{tabular}{l|r|r|r|r|r}
\hline
id & year & month & day & tmax & tmin\\
\hline
MX17004 & 2010 & 1 & 30 & 27.8 & 14.5\\
\hline
MX17004 & 2010 & 2 & 2 & 27.3 & 14.4\\
\hline
MX17004 & 2010 & 2 & 3 & 24.1 & 14.4\\
\hline
MX17004 & 2010 & 2 & 11 & 29.7 & 13.4\\
\hline
MX17004 & 2010 & 2 & 23 & 29.9 & 10.7\\
\hline

\end{tabular}

\hypertarget{a-tech-stack-for-automatically-reproduced-research}{%
\section{A Tech Stack for Automatically Reproduced Research}\label{a-tech-stack-for-automatically-reproduced-research}}

Background reading:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Gentleman and Lang (\protect\hyperlink{ref-GentlemanStatisticalAnalysesReproducible2004}{2004})
\item
  Healy (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HealyPlainPersonGuide}{n.d.})
\end{itemize}

Our goal in this chapter and the next is to assemble a tech stack for automatically reproducing research. Where Gentleman and Lang (\protect\hyperlink{ref-GentlemanStatisticalAnalysesReproducible2004}{2004}) and Healy (\protect\hyperlink{ref-HealyPlainPersonGuide}{n.d.}) focus on preparing code and papers in such a way that outputs are more likely to be \emph{reproducible}, we can use additional tools to \emph{automatically reproduce} outputs. This allows us to do things like automatically post updated preprints or online supplements.

\begin{figure}

{\centering \includegraphics[width=1\linewidth]{images/reproducibility/stack} 

}

\caption{A tech stack for automatically reproduced research.  Yellow tools are used to assemble documents from a markup language and R code.  Light green tools manage references and allow us to automatically format bibliographies.  Git and GitHub Pages, in red, combine version control and webhosting for automatically reproduced research reports.  make and Travis-CI automate the process of running a sequence of scripts.  yaml files, in purple, and R DESCRIPTION files, in aquamarine, are used to configure settings for several different tools in the stack.  A number of extensions to R Markdown, in orange, support specific kinds of complex exports, including books, websites, and package documentation.}\label{fig:stack}
\end{figure}

This chapter will focus on the \emph{document assembly} side of the stack.

\hypertarget{document-assembly}{%
\subsection{Document Assembly}\label{document-assembly}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Same basic idea as HTML or LaTeX

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Write the ``source code'' for the document in a plaintex format with markup
  \item
    Process or typeset the source code to generate the nice-looking document
  \item
    We'll be using Markdown instead of HTML or LaTeX, because it's simpler
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \emph{{[}Things to install{]}}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    LaTeX (recommended: \href{https://yihui.name/tinytex/}{TinyTeX})
  \item
    \href{https://pandoc.org/}{Pandoc} (recommended: version 2.11 or later, which handles citations more smoothly)
  \item
    \texttt{rmarkdown} R package (recommended: version 2.5 or later)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{write-in-markdown}{%
\subsubsection{Write in Markdown}\label{write-in-markdown}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \href{https://daringfireball.net/projects/markdown/}{Markdown overview}
\item
  \href{https://pandoc.org/MANUAL.html\#pandocs-markdown}{Pandoc's Markdown}
\item
  \href{https://rstudio.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/03/R\%20Markdown-reference.pdf}{R Markdown reference guide}
\end{itemize}

\emph{{[}image and URL{]}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\FunctionTok{\# Header 1 \#}
\FunctionTok{\#\# Header 2 \#\#}

\NormalTok{Here\textquotesingle{}s a paragraph. }

\NormalTok{And another, with *emphasis* and **bold**.  This sentence has a footnote.}\OtherTok{[\^{}foo]}

\OtherTok{[\^{}foo]: }\NormalTok{bar}

\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{Itemized}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{lists}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{are}
\SpecialStringTok{{-} }\NormalTok{easy}

\SpecialStringTok{1. }\NormalTok{So}
\SpecialStringTok{2. }\NormalTok{are}
\SpecialStringTok{3. }\NormalTok{enumerated}
\SpecialStringTok{4. }\NormalTok{lists}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Conversion

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{pandoc\ ex.md\ -o\ ex.pdf}
  \item
    \texttt{pandoc\ ex.md\ -o\ ex.html}
  \item
    \texttt{pandoc\ ex.md\ -o\ ex.docx}
  \item
    \href{https://pandoc.org/index.html}{More pandoc conversion formats}
  \item
    In RStudio, click ``Knitr'' or ``Preview'' (default shortcut Command-Shift-K)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{add-metadata-and-configuration-with-a-yaml-header}{%
\subsubsection{Add metadata and configuration with a YAML header}\label{add-metadata-and-configuration-with-a-yaml-header}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  YAML (\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YAML}{``YAML Ain't Markup Language''})

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \href{https://yaml.org/refcard.html}{Syntax cheat sheet}
  \item
    Used for configuration files (and headers)
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
---
author: "The Jag Cat"
title: "Let's go outside and do bird murder"
abstract: >
  Right now there are birds in the backyard.  They are hopping around and chirping and being birds.  In this paper, I argue that we should go outside and do a murder to them.  
  
geometry: "margin=1.25in"
mainfont: "Georgia"
fontsize: 11pt

toc: true
numbersections: true
colorlinks: true
---
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Available options depend on the output format
\item
  Difference between top-level configuration options and \texttt{output:pdf\_document} options
\item
  \texttt{header-includes} for any LaTeX packages you want to load
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{add-r-code-with-r-markdown}{%
\subsubsection{Add R code with R Markdown}\label{add-r-code-with-r-markdown}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \href{https://rstudio.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/03/RMarkdown-reference.pdf}{R Markdown reference guide}
\item
  \emph{Code chunks} are wrapped in \emph{fences} (3 backticks or tildes)

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    In braces: \href{https://rmarkdown.rstudio.com/lesson-5.html}{language} chunk label, \href{https://yihui.org/knitr/options/}{chunk options}
  \item
    RStudio UI helpers for chunk options

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \texttt{echo\ =\ FALSE} to hide code but still execute it
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  File extension should be \texttt{.Rmd}
\end{itemize}

\begin{verbatim}
```{r}
library(ggplot2)
foo = data.frame(x = 1:25, 
                 y = rnorm(25, mean = 1, sd = 15))
ggplot(foo, aes(x, y)) +
    geom_point()
```
\end{verbatim}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Must be assembled with \texttt{rmarkdown::render()}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \texttt{clean\ =\ FALSE}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Include R code chunks in paper?

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Pro: Shows exactly which bits of code were used for which bit of the paper
  \item
    Cons:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      LaTeX is already very slow
    \item
      Journals require separate image files
    \item
      Tension with encapsulation and a clear sequence of files for different steps of the analysis
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  An approach I currently use

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    ``Main analysis'' \texttt{.Rmd} file, generates plots and tables
  \item
    Automatically reproduced
  \item
    Knit to HTML, \href{https://dhicks.github.io/p_curve/}{hosted online}
  \item
    \texttt{paper.md} incorporates images and tables generated by analysis script
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{reference-management}{%
\subsection{Reference Management}\label{reference-management}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Recommended: \href{https://www.zotero.org/}{Zotero} with the \href{https://retorque.re/zotero-better-bibtex/}{Better Bibtex extension}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    TIL the Better Bibtex devs \href{https://retorque.re/zotero-better-bibtex/exporting/pandoc/}{recommend exporting in CSL rather than Bib(la)tex}
  \item
    I also find \href{http://zotfile.com/}{Zotfile} extremely useful.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \href{https://drive.google.com/file/d/1MuVaaSYVPBjEkYshbjG3j0YrhOmhzKGL/view?usp=sharing}{\texttt{example-refs.json}}
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\CommentTok{{-}{-}{-}}
\AnnotationTok{bibliography:}\CommentTok{ example{-}refs.json}
\CommentTok{{-}{-}{-}}\NormalTok{{-}}
\NormalTok{@YoungReliabilityEnvironmentalEpidemiology2019 have appealed to concerns about p{-}hacking }\CommentTok{[}\OtherTok{@SimonsohnPcurveKeyFiledrawer2014; @HeadExtentConsequencesPHacking2015, 3}\CommentTok{]}\NormalTok{. }
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

({\textbf{???}}) have appealed to concerns about p-hacking ({\textbf{???}}; {\textbf{???}}).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \href{https://bookdown.org/yihui/rmarkdown-cookbook/bibliography.html\#changing-citation-style}{Changing citation style}
\end{itemize}
